"On the treatment of water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter I
"Know that this illness often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife presses the child's head without gentleness.
"And sometimes it also happens for an unknown cause which is not known.
"And I myself have not seen this illness in anyone other than children, and for every one of them that I saw death followed quickly; therefore it seemed to me to abandon the treatment of it.
"And I myself saw a boy from them whose head was filled with water, and his head increased in size every day until he could no longer sit on his own because of the size of his head; and the fluid accumulated until he died.
And this moisture either gathers between the skin and bone, or it gathers beneath the bone above the sinus.
And if the operation is on that [area], and there is moisture between the skin and bone, and an abscess has formed, then it is necessary to cut one incision in the middle of the head, with a width equal to one fingerbreadth; and let the length of the incision be like two finger joints, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a trepan.
Therefore, if the moisture has increased more and the abscess is larger, then place those two incisions according to this form.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side; and when you press with your hand towards the inside, water flows out and is not hidden from you; then it is necessary to cut three incisions in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after making the incision, remove all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and wool. Then dress it on top with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and care for the wound with poultices and ointments.
And do not neglect to bind the head securely. And feed the patient with every dry food of little moisture until the part is strengthened and healed.
Another method of incision is to consider where the swelling of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is more on the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right side, or on the left side.  />
Therefore, ascend with your incision to where you see the abscess and the filling of water; and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And be careful not to cut an artery, and cause bleeding, and the patient may die from that bleeding with the evacuation of moisture.
On cutting arteries that are behind the ears.
Chapter 2
When someone has a cold affecting their eyes or chest, and it persists despite the treatment of medications: then the final cure for them is the cutting of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, the patient must have their head shaved. Then rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries become visible. Then let the patient tighten their neck with any of their own cloths. Then observe where the vein pulsates.  /> Deinde incide eam superius in medio. And the locations of both are behind the ears and slightly depressed; they are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark each with wax and cut both with a sharp scalpel on either side of the opening, and make this incision as wide as the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it from above with this incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' width.
For when the vein is cut, blood flows out in a pulsating manner, which jumps to the innermost parts with frequent leaps.
But if the artery is not perceptible by touch, then measure from the ear the distance of three fingerbreadths. Then mark with wax and find it until you reach the opening.
And what should be released from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you will release more or less than that. The entire amount according to what appears to you from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
Then observe at the wound. Then if something remains at the opening from the bandage, cut it off so that a hot abscess does not form; then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
On the extraction of temporal arteries.
Chapter 3.
When someone has an old hemicrania or acute catarrh due to sharp humors and heat in the temples, or an old, severe headache similar to these, and is treated with the methods of medical therapy but does not benefit from it; then we have found that in these illnesses extraction of the arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have narrated, is beneficial.
And the method of operation for their extraction is as follows: the patient should shave the hair from his temples and direct [his attention] to the visible artery in the temple. It is indicated to you by its pulse and rarely conceals itself except in a few people or from intense cold.
If it is hidden from you, then the patient should bind his head with any of his cloths; afterwards, rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with hot water until it appears clearly to you. Then take the spatula that I described earlier. ï¸Ž Then use it to easily scrape away the skin until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it and pull it upwards until you have drawn it out of the skin and made it exit from all the tissues beneath it on every side.
If, however, the artery is thin, then wrap it around the end of the hook. Then cut from it a portion according to how much its ends are lengthened and contracted, so that there is no hemorrhage. ï¸Ž For if it is not cut or separated, the blood does not rise up at all.
Then evacuate from blood six ounces, up to three.
If the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with a double, strong thread of silk or catgut, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the wound has healed, and blood flow occurs.
Then cut the excess between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a hot iron until the ends are cut off, using a cautery knife, and it will remain in that place for the same operation.
And this is a better procedure, unless the patient has a fever or a hot constitution. For a cautery is among those things which help to dry up humors.  /// The translator's note: "The text here has 'quorum' instead of 'quoniam', which would change the meaning to 'because of those things...' Therefore, it is a better remedy.
And after the removal of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cooked dough and well-prepared poultices placed on top.
And after it has dissolved, scrape with dry medicines that promote the growth of flesh, and with ointments, until it heals.
If during the operation there is bleeding from the artery, then hurry to cut it off with a hot iron or to fill the place with wax; and hold your hand until the blood is stopped. If none of these things are available to you, then place your finger on the spot until the blood congeals.  /// The translator's note: "The Latin text has 'donec congelentur sanguines', with a plural verb form. I have assumed that this is a scribal error and translated as if it were singular."  /// The translator's note: "In the original Arabic text, this section is numbered as chapter 270."  /// The translator's note: "The translation follows the edited Latin text of Meyerhof and Carra de Vaux (1938), with some modifications based on the reading of the manuscripts. And pierce the place with cold water until the pain subsides, and then bandage it as needed.
And to facilitate and ease the extraction of the artery, it is best to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery; let the sharpness of both blades be similar to an almagdatem. However, they are much sharper than two knives. For if they are as sharp as two knives, cold quickly overtakes both of them; and they do not cut the flesh swiftly. Therefore, since there is a little thickness in them, they hold the heat of the fire, and cut the flesh swiftly; with this, therefore, the work is better than any other work, easier, and more convenient.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be as thick as a finger after you mark two places with the cautery; and put both very hot until they reach the vein, cutting it in two places so that what is between its ends becomes long. For they do not heal completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV
Â¶ On the treatment of persistent, sharp eye-watering.
When tears are persistent and their flow is from the veins outside on top of the head, and medicines with medical treatment do not help; and you see the face of the patient already turning red, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead as if ants were walking there; and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hair is now corroded away; and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tearsâ€”then the ancients needed this procedure for their treatment and called it "treatment with a sword." â€º
And it is necessary that you advise the sick person to shave the hairs on their forehead. Then make three equally distant incisions along the length of the forehead: and let the length of each incision be like two fingers, and let one incision be in the middle of the forehead, equally distant from the length of the nose; and the second should be a little further from the movement of the shoulders; and the third should be on the other side. And take care not to cut the arteries that are on both sides; and keep your hand away from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all the incisions be like three connected fingers in size: and prepare a piece of sponge for yourself, or many dry cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a thread with two pointed ends, whose following form is this: From the incision that is near the temple, to the second incision; and with it peel off all the skin between the two incisions, along with the soft tissue that is on top of it. Then do likewise from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove that thread, and insert into the first incision again a different instrument called a sharp knife, gently from one side, not sharply from the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp edge toward the attached flesh with the skin, and its gentle side toward the bone; and push it until it reaches the middle incision; and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until what is seen of the skin.
Then do this in the middle incision until the other incision.
And when an equal amount of blood has flowed out, press on the places with clots of blood to stop the bleeding.
Then place a patch of old linen in every slit, and place upon them small balls soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see that no hot abscess occurs.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with an ointment of balm and other treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter 5
About the treatment of sharp tears and head colds affecting the eyes from within the head.
When someone has sharp, painful, constant colds, and you see their eyes are thin, small, and their sight is already weakened; and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out from them, and there is a sharp pain in the depths of the head that hurts, and sneezing follows: then know that these things come to pass only from many veins.
And a better treatment for them is this.  /> And it is that you should advise the patient to shave their forehead. Then cut one slit in the middle of the forehead, or another somewhat in width, and begin with the slit from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision reach the mouth; and draw your hand away from the temples that move during chewing until the mouth is exposed; and dry all the blood with a sponge.  />  Â·
Then separate between the two lips of the slit with old linen cloth, or with linens from flax. Then bind on top with small balls and soak the balls in wine and oil, so that no hot abscess occurs.
And when you loosen it and see that the hot abscess has lessened: then it is necessary for you to scrub the mouth until flesh begins to form there.  />
Then take care of him with a drying regimen that causes flesh to form, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of resin and prepare a plaster from them, and administer it at the time of flesh formation in these wounds.
Chapter VI
Â§ On the care for what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar, and the like; or some kind of animal.
When, therefore, a stone or something of the kind from those which do not grow and are not added falls into the ear, expose the ear to sunlight; but if you see the stone, then drip some oil of violets or sesame oil into the ear. Then administer an extraction and cause sneezing with a condiment; and plug the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you have placed around the ear fragments of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then administer the extraction with tweezers, that is, fine pincers; and their form is this.  />
If it comes out with the tweezers, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction with a fine, small-bladed hook.
But if it does not come out through that, then make a tube from metal and insert the end of the tube deeply into the opening of the ear, and plug what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there be no way for air except through the tube; then draw it out with your strong attraction. Â•
Often indeed it goes out with what we have narrated. But if it does not go out in this manner: then take from helkalimbat or from glue prepared with which birds are caught, a small portion; and put it at the end of the stick after you have wound over it a neatly placed bandage. Then insert it into the ear canal easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
Therefore if it does not go out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to cut open (the ear) before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the manner of cutting is that the physician should first open a vein on the head; and draw blood from him according to his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upward; and find a small cut in the root of the ear at its fatty part in a depressed place itself; and let the cut be lunar shaped, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instrument is possible for you. ã€‹
Then either make the cut swiftly yourself and take care of it until it heals.
If however the thing that fell into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell up, then administer an extraction for it.
But if it does not obey you to go out, then take a thin, subtle spatula according to this shape:
And apply an incision of that kind for the grains which fall into the ear and do not do this when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear until you place it in many pieces; then remove them with tweezers, or a fine probe, or suction, as we have said, for its extraction will be eased.
For water which enters the ear, it is fitting for the patient to use sneezing first of all; and now he has filled his ear with some old bandage; and he lies upon that ear in which there is water. If it exits through that part, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of your finger, thin and soft, and heat them slightly with fire; and let the sick person put one of them into his ear hole: and let him jump on one foot on that side, and strike it with another stone on the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, do not cease doing this with the stone and the stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out in such a way that you should take one of either rush or reed, and insert its tip into the ear; and set fire to the other end with a flame, until most of it is burnt. Then repeat this with another rush, and do it many times until all the water comes out; and remove it with a probe as previously mentioned for the stone.
But if an animal enters it, and its body is small, like a flea, then care for it as I said in the section on division.
But if it has a large body, which is perceptible to the senses; then administer its extraction with tweezers and hooks: and this case is easier than all others that are retained in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm born in the ear, when you care for it with what we said in division and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not help; then you must examine the ear in sunlight. ã€‹ If something from the worms is visible there, then remove it with tweezers or fine hooks.
But if nothing is visible there, then take a probe of this shape, narrow at the bottom and wide at the top; and insert its thin tip into the ear according to how much the patient can tolerate; then draw with it strongly. For this, often, until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then fill the circle of the probe with wax, as I said about the stone.
If they do not come out with what I said: then administer the distillations that the ancients have already experienced in the killing of worms; and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring be that with which you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is drawn here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, narrow at the bottom, in which there should be a small hole, wide at the top.
Let, therefore, the plunger that is inside the cavity of the probe be made from bronze, well-crafted. And if you wish, take a reed and wrap the tip of it neatly with cotton; then pour oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the probe; and let the probe be in the ear; then insert the reed with the cotton on top and press it gently with your hand until the oil is pushed into the cavity of the ear, and the patient feels it inside; and what is poured into the ear should already be warmed a little by fire; and be careful that it is not very cold, for medicines do not tolerate that. Chapter 7.
Â¶ On the treatment of gold impaction.
Sometimes some infants come out of their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it has also happened to some people that an impaction occurs in the ear canal from a wound or flesh born there.
And this impaction sometimes occurs deep within the ear canal, which sight cannot follow; and sometimes it happens near the outer part of the canal, and sight follows it.
That which occurs in the deep part of the ear and is not seen by the eyesight is difficult to heal in many cases.
And in that which follows sight, you should place the ear of the patient in the sun and look at it; then if you see a visible obstruction, open it with a fine probe, the shape of which is as follows: its tip should have a small width, part of which is sharp, and the rest of the probe should be smooth on two sides so that it does not harm the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is formed of flesh within the ear, then hold it with a fine hook and cut it with great ease until all the flesh is removed.
But if the obstruction is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth probe and heat it with fire for a short while; then insert it into the opening of the ear.
If therefore you feel the obstruction along with what the patient finds with a heavy hearing, endeavor to open it with ease; and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.  ///
Then place a linen bandage over the ear according to the size of the opening, rolled up in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure about the healing of the wound.
Or take a linen cloth and soak it in rose water; then sprinkle ground alum over it and use that.
But if you see the ear swelling already, then it is necessary to remove the linen cloth and leave it with another linen cloth that you will roll up in a plaster made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides; then treat it until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and place the ear on it; and use the rest of the treatment which cuts off the aforementioned bleeding in the section on distillations.
In the care of ptosis of the eyelids.
Chapter Eight.
These ptoses that occur in the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with tweezers or with a hook and cut them from their roots with a scraper. Then place ground cinnabar on the places.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lenticular cautery.
And it is better to cauterize them after the incision. For often they return when something remains of their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Â¶ On the care of chalazion in the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter Nine.
It often happens that something appears in the eyelids similar to a chalazion in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is named 'grain'; it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation for them is that you should then consider if there is a 'grain' emerging from what appears on the eyelid and it moves in all directions, and its nature is easy.  ?>
Therefore find above it a slit of suitable width. Then excise it from all sides easily until it comes out. Then hang it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its removal is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight incision, then it may harm the patient somewhat.
If the slit is large, then close it with stitches and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. The ointment restores it and fleshes it out.
And if the gravel has slipped into the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then turn back the eyelid and hook out the gravel with a hook; besides this you will need to make an incision and press it from all sides.
And if the eyelid is stretched by the incision, this does no harm.
Then after the incision of the gravel, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the place with what makes it fleshy until the patient is healed.

Chapter 10: Concerning the treatment for inflammation of the eyelids. Trachoma is a fat that is generated in the tissues of the upper eyelid; and it occurs most often in children. Â¶ Concerning the types of ataxia of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye becomes lax, so that it blinks as it should not.

When hairs are born in the eyelids of the eye beyond their natural course among the natural hairs and they grow old; then these [hairs] harm the eye and cause various diseases to occur, such as constant tearing, laxity of the eyelids, and opacities, until it leads to the destruction of the eye.

When this entropion happens to the eye, it is called leporine.

About the care for alcatrat that occurs on the lower eyelid, which is a wrinkle or contraction upward to the right and left.
Chapter XIV
This alcatrat that occurs below is what is truly called alcatrat. And it is natural and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or tear, or branding, and similar things.
And the way to operate on it is that you take a needle in which there is a double thread, and dip it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the skin upward with a needle and cut it with a broad scalpel.  /> Therefore, if the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, that is good.  />
And if not, take a thread and place it in the tear, and turn the eyelid with it; and find two tears on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two tears be from the two corners of the incision until they meet and form a corner: until when they are gathered together this shape is formed, which is in the form of the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that skin to the extent that its lower sharp side extends from where the eyelid follows. Then bring together the separated parts, or sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said about softening ointments and compresses until they heal.
And if it happens to the Xatrati due to a tear or stitching, or cauterization, then you should split the tear smoothly under the eyelashes, just as was mentioned before. Then separate between the two lips with a probe, as we have said.
And the main point about caring for the Xatrati, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Xatrati.
For it is of many different forms: and a skilled craftsman prepares his ingenuity in whatever way is possible for him, until he makes the figure return to its natural form or very close to it, and the patient himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in numerous arrangements.
Chapter 15.
About the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
It sometimes happens to many people that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, you should insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up, or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula that is not sharp like other spatulas, but rather blunt slightly, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed and does not harm the eye with its sharpness. And your incision should be as if you were easily excising that adhesion until, when the eyelid returns to its natural form, the entire adhesion heals.
Then irrigate the eye with salty water and wash it with plain water, or distill into it from a sieve made of crushed juniper berries mixed with water.
Then separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen probe. And place over the eye a wool compress soaked in egg white. And on the third day use a sieve and patches until it heals.
Of the incision of the nail and prominence of the tear-duct flesh.

In cutting Alguardenic and the flesh that grows on the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in the eyes of certain people a red fleshy growth appears, either composed or laid on top, until it covers the pupil or approaches and presses upon the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles the stem of a rose.

And do similarly with additional flesh that happens to appear in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that a stranger should perform the cure of every weakened eye either by natural means or by accidental means, operating with iron; and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that this member can bear that operation.
About collecting seed from the eye.
Chapter Eighteen.
The seed are red veins that cover the eye and prevent it from functioning properly: they weaken the eye over time.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye with the seed is strong and has no other disease besides the seed: then collect the seed from that eye.
And instruct the patient to place their head in your lap. ï¸
Then hang those veins with one or two hooks according to your discretion; and let the hook be thin and double-edged, like this shape, or there may be two hooks on a single body, like this shape.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors carefully, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and loosened; and be careful not to damage the eye with the tips of the forceps.
And let your procedure be at midday in the sunlight, and be very steady in your work: so that you do not cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then drop into the eye a green or red juice: so that its sharpness can corrode what remains of the seed.
But if you are not able to collect all of it in that time, then apply a bandage to the eye with something that will soothe the pain of the eye; and leave him for several days until the pain subsides and is released from the warm swelling: then repeat on him according to the same method until he is healed. ï¸ â€

About reducing a broken nose.
Chapter Nineteen.
Doctors call the broken nose a fistula, which you treat with a cautery or sharp medicine that burns, as its previous account has described.
And if it does not heal, then there is no cure for it; unless you pierce above the abscess at its birthplace, and draw out all the moisture and pus that is in it until the mouth (of the abscess) is uncovered.
When therefore the mouth is uncovered and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument of this shape.
And it is called "Rough Cap.
Let it be made of Indian iron and let its head be round like a needle and have the punctuation of a file or very fine awl puncturation, and place it on the corrupted bone site.
Then turn it between your fingers and press with your hand a little while until you know that the corruption is already scraped off; and do this several times.
Then restore the site with drying, styptic medicines.
If the site then becomes solid and flesh grows on it, and the flow of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days without renewing, or forming an abscess, and nothing else happens to it, then know that it has already been healed.
And if not, then there is no cure for it, unless you bring the fistula to the nostril opening, in this way.
And it is, that you uncover the bone with a knife or sharp medicine; and when the mouth is uncovered, then take a borer of this shape, its tip should be iron and triangular; and let its wood be a tapered pyramid up to the tip as you see.  ()
Then place it on the mouth itself; and let it be near the lacrimal; and move your hand a little away from the eye. Then turn your hand with the borer until it penetrates the mouth and the patient feels it; by this means he finds wind coming out of him when he places his hand on his nose.
Therefore, then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I have taught you.
When the flesh is born and the place becomes hardened, then the material that was flowing out to the outside returns to the nose; therefore it is lighter on the sick person.
Â¶ On the Reduction of Protrusion of the Eye.
Chapter 20
When the eye protrudes completely but there is no damage or decrease in vision, then it is necessary that you release the sick person; afterwards bleed him; then place cupping glasses on his neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply to the eye a salve made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and colophony. Then bind the eye on top of the plaster with many little poultices.
And the method of making poultices is as follows: take many soft cloths and double each one over four times; make many of them; then place one on top of another according to the amount that the eye needs; then bind them with a hard cloth tightly.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the bandage becomes soft again, then it is necessary to bind it tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we have said.
If you see the eye has returned to its normal appearance, that is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, poultices, bandage, and cupping glasses until it heals.
Â¶ On the Incision of the Eye's Tunic
Chapter 21
When a disruption occurs in the eye's tunic and it protrudes from the eyelids like a grain of the eye, and it disfigures the appearance of the person because of this, and you wish to incise it, then the operation on it is as I will narrate: and it is, that you introduce a needle in the root of the eye from below to above.
Then introduce another needle with twice as much thread from the tear duct, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle in place according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie it around the edge of the vein in a circle; and tighten it very much.
Then remove the needle, and place on the eye a piece of wool soaked in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread falls out with the vein.

Then it is pierced with a fine needle so that it reaches to the depth of the eye, and the white moisture runs out, and the eye descends; then tie it immediately; then tighten it until it heals.
Â§ On the treatment of trachoma.
Chapter 22
This disease called trachoma is nothing but pus that gathers in the eye, similar to water dripping down; and it is not itself.
And the method of working on it is as follows: make the patient sit on a prepared or straight seat. Â•
Then take his head with both your hands and move it until you see the pus come to the lower parts of your eyes. Then it remains still and does not go down, and the light is released; and the patient sees things as he used to see them; and if it has not gone below, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus does not prepare with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine needle and pierce with it above the small piece of skin for a short time at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal membrane, until the pus comes out.
When he exits, then drip warm water into the eye to which you have added honey; or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Â§ On the care of water that has descended into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have already mentioned the types of water in the division, and that which is suitable for making an opening, as declared and expounded by speech, then take the same according to health.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to make the sick person sit between your hands facing the window towards the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and tie it very tightly; then with your right hand, hold the eyelid of his eye if the eye containing water is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take almagda in your right hand if the eye is left; or in your left hand, if the eye is right.
Then place the end of the almagda near the crown with a thickness equal to that of a ray on the white part of the eye where the tear duct appears; then push the almagda with force; and you turn your hand with it, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the almagda has already reached an empty space. Â¶
And the measure of the journey of the almagda to the depth should be the measure of the space that is from the pupil until the end of the blackness; and this is the crown of the eye. For you will see it in the very pupil of the eye's vision because of the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then place the almagda to the point where the water is located. Then press it down with one force after another. Â¶
If the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that immediately on which he opens his eyesight, and almagda in his eye.
Then rest for a little while. Therefore, if the water descends, then remove it secondly, except that almagda comes out.
Therefore, when it sticks and does not rise, then remove the almagda easily; and you slowly turn your hand.
Then dissolve some clear rock salt in water and wash the eye with it inside. Then place outside the eye the entire flax or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, and bind it with a healthy eye.
We, however, and those of our time, place on it ground barley with egg white.
But if the almagda does not obey you when entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a human whose eye is very hard, then you must take an instrument called "spatamile," which looks like this:
Therefore, prick with it only the conjunctiva and do not extend your hand into the opening; and you should make a subtle path to the almagda's place.
Then introduce the almagda, as we have said.
And at the end of your work, let there be prepared for the sick person a well-made bed, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark house; and he shall be kept from all movements and from coughing; and give him food by which his nature is soothed; and let him not move his head at all to the right or left.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then remove it in that dark house, and test his eyesight; and make him see things. ã€‹ Then bring back the ligament until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he do this at the time of the treatment or immediately after the incision; rather, it should be avoided because water rises quickly through a strong gaze.
But if a hot aposteme occurs, then it is necessary to loosen the eye before the seventh day and correct it with something that will calm the aposteme until it calms down; then absolve the sight of it, and place an orale on its face to regulate the sight under it for several days, while he remains in that dark house.
Then let him go out from the house gradually, and turn to his usual activities.
And know that in salt water, the student is not excused from frequent vision; then he may dare to proceed with the operation.
And indeed there came to us a certain one of those who are from Aleppo, who said that in Aleppo a magdan was pierced, through which water is sucked out.
But truly I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this; nor have I read of it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from the magdan, as if one who does not know them were to stand over it.
They should be made properly of brass; and their ends should have the same subtlety as those that are triangular and pointed.
Chapter XXIV
Â¶ On the care of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes various fleshes are born in the nose, among which there is something that resembles a scorpion with many feet; and from this is a cancerous flesh, stone-like, dark in color; and from this is a gentle flesh, not dark in color.
In those cases where the flesh is soft, not deceiving, and not cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands facing the ground. Open his nose and throw the hook into those fleshes; then draw them out, and cut what you have grasped from them with a fine sharp spoon on one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
If something remains that cannot be cut off, then scrape it with a fine, sharp instrument until nothing remains of it. But if the bleeding is severe or a hot abscess occurs, then apply what is necessary: if it is one of the malignant abscesses, hurry to cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is removed.
Then throw vinegar, water, or wine into the nose after cutting. But if the nose is opened and fluid comes out as far as the palate, then know that he is already cured.
But if the fluid does not penetrate as it should, then know that there is flesh formed on the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the cutting.
Then it is necessary for you to introduce a thread of linen with some thickness and many knots tied in it, and place between each knot the size of a finger or less. The patient should then incline his head so that he can send one end of the thread into the nose with a rod or something similar after making it like a needle: he should draw out his nasal mucosa until it reaches the cavity, and comes out over his throat.
And indeed, similar to this are the tricks that boys do in school: it is an easy thing for someone who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread: one end that comes out over the nose and the other that remains inside the nose. Then the seamstress sews up the flesh with knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the skin is already tied with knots of thread.
Then remove the thread; and put on the nose after washing away the blood an ointment that you have soaked in Egyptian oil: do this for three days or more, until all the ointment has eaten away what remains of the flesh.
Then at the end of the process, place a lead pipe into the nose for several days until it heals.
If, however, dry treatment is needed, use that.
For making a head-purifier with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or copper, similar to a small lamp, open like a shell; and its opening likewise.
And if you wish, make a closed pipe, like a reed.
And the opening of the instrument for making a head-purifier should be full and open; it should have a handle at its end with which you hold it when you heat oil or whatever you wish in it from juices and meltable things.
Chapter 25.
Often a polyp arises at the tip of the nose and increases and grows with time until its appearance becomes ugly.  />  />  />
And because of that it is necessary to cut it off at the beginning of its appearance and root out all of it. Then place on its site either a caustic or a burning medicine that will remain in its place.
If therefore its cutting is delayed until it grows larger, then examine whether it is stony, hard, dark in color, with little feeling; then do not touch it with iron because the abscess is malignant.
I have often seen that when someone gets a boil, a great evil befalls him who has it.
And if the boil is soft to touch, not black in color, and you see that it can be cut entirely, then evacuate the patient and cut it without fear or hesitation: and treat the place with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter XXVI
Â¶ On suturing the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a break occurs in one of these cartilages, operation is of little benefit, except in certain people.
Therefore, when something like this happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood: then apply dressings to the wound along with the stitching; and then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the separation have already been separated and each part is healthy: then you should scrape off all parts from their skin until they bleed. Then join the two lips with stitching and tighten them, and sprinkle over the blood powder of dragon's blood and crushed frankincense; and place on top of the powders a piece of adhesive plaster and some other from the consolidating plasters: and leave it tied for two days or three. Â• Then release it, and change the medication; and leave it until the threads dissolve by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of stitching is to join the separation either with needles, as we have described for stomach stitching; or join the separation with thread, as I have taught you.

Chapter 29
"About filing teeth with iron.
"Sometimes rough and ugly scabs form on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and out, between the gums, which may blacken, turn yellow or green until corruption reaches the gum, making the teeth foul and unsightly.
"Therefore, you should make the patient sit between your hands, with his head on your lap, and file the tooth where you see scabs and something like sand; continue until nothing remains of them. Do the same for blackening, greening, yellowing, and other discolorations until they are cleansed.
If therefore that which is in them is removed from the beginning of the reason, it is good; and if not, then repeat the reasoning over them on another day, second, and third, until at last the study reaches its desired end.
And know that the tooth requires razors of diverse shapes and many figures, according to which it is prepared for your operation; therefore, the razor with which one shaves the tooth from below is different from the razor with which one shaves between the teeth, according to other shapes.

Chapter XXX
Â¶ On the Extraction of Tooth Pain
It is necessary that you cure the tooth from its pain with all skill and be slow in extracting it. For its replacement cannot be supplied when it is extracted, because it is a high substance; therefore, there can be no excuse for extracting it, unless when the patient is forced to extract it in order to strengthen and confirm the aching tooth to you.
For many times the pain deceives the patient and makes him think that it is in the tooth and he extracts it; then the pain does not subside until the sick tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this from the practice of bloodletting.
When the aching tooth itself is confirmed to you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a lancet, on which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened from all parts; then move it with your fingers or with fine forceps little by little, until you shake it out.  />
Then place large forceps on it in a good position, and hold the patient's head between your knees so that you have already held it well, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it under the object from all sides with ease, and attempt to move it as you did initially.
However, if the tooth is perforated or decayed, then you should fill that hole with cloth and tie it with a fine thread so that it does not break during your constriction on it with forceps.
And you must carefully make an incision all around the gum from every side very much.
And be careful not to break it, and let a part of it remain, so that the worse evil returns upon the patient with greater pain than before.
And beware of doing what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and audacity when extracting it, beyond what we have described to administer.
For often they cause great illnesses to happen to people, which are more difficult to break and all its roots remain, or some of them; or they extract it with some bones of the jaw, as we have often witnessed this.
Then it should be washed after extraction with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If there is bleeding from the site (for this often happens), then grind some zegi and fill the site with it; if not, cauterize it if zegi does not help you.
The form of the fine forceps with which the tooth is initially moved should have long tips and a short handle, and be thick so that they do not bend when pressed on the tooth; as you see in large-handled forceps, until when you tie them, they do not give way or bend; of short tips, and made from Indian steel or well-made from faled steel with straight tips: and at their tips there should be teeth, some of which fit into others and bind firmly.
And sometimes the extremities resemble the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are very constricted.
Chapter 31
About the removal of teeth, and the extraction of broken jawbones.
When something remains after the extraction of a broken tooth, then it is necessary to place a poultice on that place with butter for one day, or two days, until the place softens; then introduce into it algasti, or forceps, whose extremities resemble the beak of a bird called a stork. The shape of these is that of forceps.
Their extremities should be made like a file inside, or like tweezers.
If it does not obey you in leaving with these forceps, then it is necessary that you make a hole in the root, and expose all the flesh, and introduce an instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Its extremity should be short, slightly thick; and it should not be made wet, so that it does not break.
If the root comes out through this, it is good; and if not, help must be given with these other instruments whose forms are these. â– 
The form of the first is a triangle, with thickened extremities.
The form is that of a subtle triangular extremity. â– 
And with other instruments and tools that have been mentioned in the section on teeth.
It can also be helped with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, a triangular twisted extremity, with some thickness so that it does not break; and it should not be made wet.
And know that there are many instruments of teeth; and likewise the other instruments are not counted, and a skilled craftsman finds for himself an instrument suitable for his work and the diseases themselves. â–¡ â– 
Because there are diseases for which the ancients did not name an instrument due to the diversity of their kinds.
But if a bone is broken from the jaw, or other bones of the mouth, or putrefies: then search over it with that which suits it among the instruments and forceps that I mentioned for extracting roots, and aid with this probe, whose form is thus.
Let there be in it something of thickness gradually, so that the bone is caught and held with it, and does not flee and wander, until the bone exits: and restore the place with suitable medicines for that.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction and care for it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII
On the Sawing of Prominent Teeth above Others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an ugly appearance is made by that; and especially when this happens to women and girls, that is, those who have been sold (i.e., married off).
Therefore, you must consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or its sawing and filing are possible: then extract it; but if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is thus.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of Indian steel, very sharp at the end.
And let your cutting, by which you cut it, be done over many days, because of the hardness of the tooth and so that another tooth is not moved.
But if it is prominent and suitable for filing, then file it with a file from India, whose form is thus.
It is entirely from India and its handle is of very small points, like the file with which needles are made: by which the tooth is filed little by little over many days easily, so that the tooth does not move, and falls; then smooth it, in the end scrape it with certain rasps.
If it is a tooth, from which a piece breaks off and injures the tongue, then one must smooth it also, until the roughness of that break is removed, and made even, and smoothed, so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
About pulling teeth with gold wire.
When a shock and blow happens to the front teeth from a strike or accident, and the patient cannot hold together what they eat so that it does not fall out, and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the cure has not been effective, then there is skill in this: to tie them with gold wire. For silver turns green and putrefies after days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be medium in fineness and thickness according to what fits between the teeth.
And the form of the pulling is thus: take a wire and insert its double between two sound teeth; then weave with the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you come with the thread to the sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the part where you began; and hold your hand with fineness and wisdom, until it is not at all removed; and let your constriction by which you tie the wire be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not escape.
Then cut the superfluous ends of the wire with scissors; and gather together both [ends] and turn them with a pincer; and hide them between the sound tooth and the loose tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it be left thus tied as long as it remains.
But if it loosens or is cut, tie them with another wire, and they will remain thus for the entire time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the pulling of two sound teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after the incident and remain there as we have said; only a skilled and experienced craftsman can accomplish this.
And sometimes a piece of cattle bone is prepared: it is shaped like a tooth and placed in the socket, where it is secured and fastened as we said: and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either congenital, with which a person is born, or acquired, from an old healed wound.
And the operation on it is this: open the mouth of the patient, placing his head in your lap and raise his tongue; then cut through that nerve-like ligament across its width until the tongue is released from its constriction.
But if there is some hardness and knotting due to the healed wound, then thrust a hook into it and search through its width until you cut the ligament and free the knot.
And be careful not to make a deep flesh cut and sever an artery there, causing blood flow.
Then wash the site of the incision with rose water or vinegar or cold water. Â•
Then place a linen cloth under the tongue which the patient must hold every night: so that it does not heal again.
But if there is bleeding, then put ground alum on the site. But if the bleeding overcomes you, then cauterize the site with a suitable cautery; then treat him with the rest of the treatment until he is cured.
Chapter 35
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function; and perhaps it grows so much that it fills the mouth.
And the procedure for this is to open the mouth of the patient facing the sun and observe the abscess.
If therefore you see it to be a dark or black color, hard, and the patient feels no pain from it; then do not touch it, because it is a cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness and has moisture; then insert a hook into it and cut it with a fine scalpel, freeing it from all parts. But if blood overcomes you during your procedure, then place ground cumin on it until the bleeding stops; then return to your procedure until you extract it with its entirety.
Then let it be rinsed with vinegar and salt; and care for the remainder of the appropriate treatment until it heals.
Â¶ On the treatment of abscesses in the two tonsils.
Chapter 36
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat and tonsils, which resemble cotton balls that appear externally; these are called two tonsils. When you treat them with what we have said about incision and they do not heal, then observe.
If therefore the abscess is hard, dark in color, and causes little pain; then do not touch it with an iron (scalpel).
And if it is red in color and its root is thick; then do not touch it even with an iron, fearing excessive bleeding. Rather, leave it until it matures. ã€‹ And then either you will pierce it or it will burst by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is fine; then you should cut into it.
And the procedure is as follows: consider before the operation whether the abscess has become hot and filled with quietness, or if it has diminished somewhat. Then have the patient sit in front of the sun, and his head should be in your lap. Open his mouth, and let the servant take him between his hands, and press his tongue downward with this instrument.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which should be as thin as a knife.
So when you press it against the tongue, and the abscess is revealed to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and insert it into the tonsil, and draw it out as far as possible to the exterior, except that you pull some of the membranes with it.  />
Then cut it with an instrument of this form which follows. It resembles scissors; however, its extremities should be curved so that the opening of each one is directly at the tip of the other. Therefore, ensure that it is made of Indian steel or steel that has been quenched in oil.
But if this instrument is not available, then cut it with a spatula of this form. Let one end be sharp, the other not.
And after you have cut one tonsil, cut the other in a similar manner.
Then, after the cutting, let the patient gargle with cold water or with vinegar and water.  />  />  />  />  />  />  />
But if bleeding occurs, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled, or myrtle leaves and other hemostatics similar to them, until the flow of blood is stopped; then care for him until he heals.
And sometimes abscesses arise in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them therefore as I said for the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have treated a woman from an abscess that had developed in her throat, which was pulling it to darkness, with little sensation and had almost closed her throat; and the woman was breathing through narrowed breath, and it had already prevented her from eating food or drinking water; and she would have reached death if it had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches until they emerged at the two nostrils.
I hastened quickly, therefore, and fixed a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large piece was drawn out from it.
Then I cut it off where it was caught in the nostril. ï¸
Then I did to her what had emerged from the other nostril's opening.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess itself; then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood ran out from it; thus the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took some food.
I did not stop cutting from that abscess many times over a long period; and the abscess followed in the place where I had cut, until it was greatly elongated with her.
Therefore, I cauterized the abscess in the throat.
It stopped growing further; then I left her and did not know what God did with her after me.
Â¶ On the incision of the abscess on the uvula.
Chapter 37.
When the cold (catarrh) descends to the uvula and becomes abscessed and is long; then it is called "perpendicular". And if it is thick and round at its lower part, then it is called "grape".
When it is treated with what we mentioned during the division, and the treatment does not help: and you see that the hot tumor has subsided from it, and is thin: then you must cut into it.
And if something round has grown from it, and it has no length, and is red or dark in color, and there is no sensation in it: then you should avoid cutting into it. For there is fear regarding the patient.
Therefore, when you see it according to the way I said, from its whiteness and length, make the patient sit before the sun; then press his tongue with the instrument that preceded in the narrative; afterwards, insert a hook into the vein and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments that I mentioned in the cutting of the two tonsils.
And you should not cut from it except what is added beyond the natural limit without adding to it.
For if you cut more than this, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting, use that which we have mentioned in the cutting of the two tonsils; and care for it until it heals.
But if the patient is frightened by the cutting, then you should use skill in cauterizing it without fear and dread.
And the method of cautery for it is not except with sharp water; and it is that you place the patient's head on your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Then take from the water that I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterizing, and make a cup of it that is not extinguished, and place it not thick nor thin, and fill with it the hollow of this instrument, whose form is this.
Let its end where the medicine is placed have a concavity like the concavity of a spoon.
And place the instrument with medicine on the wound itself; and let the patient lie on their side, so that saliva may flow from their mouth due to the internal medicine, lest anything should descend into the throat and harm them.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should place it on the wound for half an hour, until you see that it has turned black and the grinding of the medicine is calmed.
And if you wish to use cotton, roll it around the tip of a finger and soak it with medicine. Then insert the finger along with the cotton into a small tube, until the cotton adheres to the wound.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you want from cauterizing the wound. Then leave it; for it dries up and falls off after three or four days.
If you need to renew the medicine, renew it; and after cauterizing, clean around the wound with cotton soaked in butter, and cleanse it with what remains of the medicine around it, and rinse with cold water.
And let it be scraped externally with ointments, and internally with gargles, until it heals.
This method of treatment is therefore healthier for the cut and further from danger.
And sometimes the wound is also treated with something even more delicate than cautery and incision; it is treated with fumigation in this manner. Take calamint, hyssop, oregano, rue, marjoram, chamomile, southernwood, and similar herbs; gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and let them be submerged in vinegar and boil; and let the pot be well sealed with clay; and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which place a concave instrument in this manner.
"Make from silver or copper and insert the end with the pellet into the patient's mouth until steam rises to the lips above the tube until the lips are much darkened.
Then repeat over it many times until it dries out.
And beware not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's arrival. For it often adds to the swelling, and it is not fitting that you do this except at the decline of its heat.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a tube and place on its end the shell of an egg so that the patient's mouth does not burn, for the eggshell prevents the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment with safety.
Â§ On the Extraction of Thorns and Bones from the Throat
Chapter 38
Many times a bone or a fishbone sticks in the throat, or something else.
Therefore, you must extract whatever is apparent from these things after pressing the tongue with the instrument toward the light, and it will be revealed to you what is in the throat.

"The ulcer is larger than the radius for a short time, and at its end there is curvature, which it enters into its throat with ease; and he should have his head raised up.

"About the Extraction of a Leech
Chapter 39
"When a leech is treated with what I said in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then look in the patient's throat in the presence of sunlight or in sunlight after you press his tongue with the instrument that I have told you about.
"If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook or with fine tweezers.
"But if it is not possible with them, then take a hollow reed and insert it into the patient's throat until it reaches the leech; then insert into the hollow of the reed a heated iron with fire. "Do this many times, and let the patient abstain from water all day.
