Then pour the fumigation and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the tube and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the patient endure thirst and eat salted and garlic foods, and not drink water.
It is therefore necessary that it will exit with this diet.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is drawn from the throat when it falls upon the very sight.
It is similar to tongs, as you see: except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. And its end is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, on which there is the roughness of a file; when it grasps something, it does not let it go at all.
Â§ On the opening and sectioning of apostemes, and their general care.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of apostemes, of various types, as will be recalled in this book; and there are two ways to open them and cut them out.
One is from the nature of the aposteme itself, and from that which it contains of humors. ï¸
And the second method is because of the places in the body where they occur.
Because an aposteme occurring in the head is different from one occurring in the anus; and an aposteme occurring in a fleshy place is different from one occurring in joints; and each of them has its own judgment based on their location.
And of aposteme (abscess) there are those which should not be pierced unless after the maturation of pus in them and its completion; and there are those which should be pierced while they are fresh and do not mature according to their due course.
Like apostemes (abscesses) that are close to joints.
For an apostema (abscess) that occurs near a joint and its duration is prolonged until it putrefies, which is in its circumference, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; therefore, it is the cause of the contraction of that limb.
Or it is an aposteme (abscess) in the main limb; for if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that or the main limb itself.
Or it is near the anus; therefore, perforate it while it is immature and not fully digested, except for a raw aposteme (abscess), unless it is near the anus so that it does not putrefy deep and reach the interior of the anus and become a fistula, or end in something that does not heal. ã€‹
You should know the hour of perforating an aposteme (abscess) that has matured according to its course; and it is at the remission of pain, the subsiding of fever, and the diminishing of redness, pulsation, and the sharpness of the head of the abscess, and other signs. ã€‹ ã€‹
And you should make the same perforation in the lower part of the aposteme (abscess) and in the more prominent swelling; and the perforation should go along the length of the body if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of the upper arms, tendons, nerves, arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those that are reflected, proceed with a perforation along that membrane.
When, however, the abscess is in fleshy parts: then it is better to leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said at the end.
For if you perforate it beforehand, the course of the discharge from it is prolonged, and there is much filth and filthiness, and perhaps its edges become hardened, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body as needed, or according to what that part requires.
And you should administer a small perforation for small abscesses and a large perforation or multiple cuts similar to the size of the abscess for large ones.
And there are abscesses for which the skin must be hollowed out and cut when it has already been made like a cloth and its end has died, as often happens with doublets and buboes.
And among them are those that are cut with a three-angled incision. And among them is the one that is cut like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And among them are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones from other incisions, are administered.  />
However, that which has no head, like the soft and superficial abscesses, should be perforated with a superficial or flat perforation only.
And when the abscess is thick and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, do not hurry and extract all the pus at that time; rather, extract a part of it from it. Then pack the abscess until another day. Then extract something from the pus again.
Do this many times according to the degree, until it comes out; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old decrepit person: for the vital spirit of the animal is often resolved from the animal with the coming out of the pus; and perhaps the patient will die, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, beware this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you perforate these boils, it is necessary that you clean the wound; and be careful. If therefore the boil is small, or if it is a flat section, then apply dressings of linen, or old cotton. And if the boil is large, and there are many fissures from the perforations, it is necessary then to insert a dressing into each fissure, until they come together.
And if in the boil you have cut off a part of the skin, or concaved it around; then it is necessary that you fill it with old cotton, or as when a cloth is woven, or shorn of linen wool without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then discard it, and treat it with that which is necessary from an ointment, until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after the cloth has soaked in them: and place it on the site of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer a regimen and care and powders, which I have described in many places of this book of ours, and in the section on division.
But if it is during the winter and the site of the boil is of many nerves; then it is necessary that you pour hot powders with wine and oil on them: and place them on the sites.
And if it is summer and the places are fleshy: then you should place small cloths soaked in water and oil, or with wine and oil, and all of that cold. Until the third day, as we said, then you should release the abscess and cleanse it, and administer to its care what is suitable for it, until it heals.
Therefore, this is what is necessary for the universal knowledge of the treatment of abscesses.
According to the second half of the division, I have already spoken about every abscess, how there is a way to its cure individually and in chapters.
Chapter 41.
Â¶ About the incision of abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
Abscesses occur on the skin of the head and are small in size; they are of the type called nodules, enclosed by tunics which are bags for them, as if they were eggs of a hen.
And their types are many.
Among them are fatty ones; and among them are those that contain a moisture like clay; and among them are those that contain a moisture like crushed grains or poultices, and similar substances; and among them are hard stony ones.
And in the incision of all of these and their removal there is no danger, as long as during their incision a vein does not cause a contrary action.
And the operation in their incision is as follows: you should test them with an instrument, whose form will be shown afterwards, which is called a probe, until you know what it contains.
For if that which it contains is moisture, then cut it with a lengthwise incision with a shallow incision in this manner.
And begin with the incision from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture has been evacuated, then remove the bag that contains that moisture, and do not leave anything of it at all.
Indeed, it often returns if something of it remains.
Then submerge the cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day. For it will eat away what remains of the tumor.
Then repeat the application of Egyptian ointment on the cotton a second and third time, and if necessary, continue doing so until you are confident that nothing remains of the tumor; then treat the wound with the ointment until it heals.
If the tumor contains a fatty lump, then cut it in the following manner, making a cross-shaped incision; and throw away the hooks between the edges of the incision, and excise it from all sides; and strive with your skill to extract the membrane that contains it.
But if a blood vessel opposes you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
And do likewise with a stone-like tumor, through incision and the same treatment, as we have said.
The incision on a stone-like tumor is easier, for it has little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are certain of these tumors which are on the head in some people, and with which there is no moisture at all.
Which is to say: for I incised a tumor on the head of an old woman, and found the tumor like a solid, hard, rough, white stone that could not be broken; and if anyone struck it by throwing something at it, they would wound themselves.
But in other tumors occurring on the head, besides these particular tumors which occur on children's heads and at the roots of the ears, the incision for all of them should be a superficial one.
Place the perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the course of the pus is relieved to the lower part.
Then treat it with what suits them from the treatment.
Â§ On the incision of abscesses in the neck.
Chapter 42.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and under the armpits and in the groins; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And among those that occur in the neck are one and many; and they generate from each other; and every abscess among them is within its own proper tunic, just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have said. ã€‹
And there are many types of these abscesses. Among them are stony ones, and among them are those that contain liquids, and among them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, when you see one among them that is of good disposition by touch, whose appearance is close to the color of the skin and moves toward every part, and which is not attached to the nerves of the neck nor to the windpipe nor to the artery, and is not deep; it is necessary that you incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along line b. to line g., and excoriate it from all sides; and spread the two lips of the skin with hooks or with many hooks if you need them, as we have said regarding the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is cautious to ensure that no vein or nerve is cut.
And let the knife not be very sharp, so that it does not make your hand slip during the incision or trouble the patient, and you do not cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery and it prevents you from operating, then place ground-up gum or certain powders that curdle the blood in the wound, and bind the wound, and leave it alone until the sharpness of the abscess subsides and the wound softens; or it begins to want to putrefy. ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹
And the blood is cut off by the tunica. Return then to your operation until you complete it.
Then explore with your index finger if there is any remnant of other small strophulae: cut them and clean them out.
If the strophula is rooted in a large vein, you should not cut it from its root. Instead, bind it with a strong double thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton that you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then care for him (the patient).
If the entire strophula is cut off, you should gather together the two lips of the wound and suture it immediately, after seeing that there is no longer any excess fluid.
However, if you see that there is still excess fluid due to the size of the strophula, you should then aim to cut its root and remove it; and apply the suture as we have said.
And those strophulae which contain humors should be perforated with a superficial perforation, so that the place of their maturity may appear to you; and make the perforation towards what is below in the body, as we have said.
Then after perforation, apply linen with ointment and similar things so that it may consume what remains of the corruption; and when you see that the wound is already clean, then care for him with a flesh-generating ointment until he heals.
Chapter 43.
About the incision of the epiglottis due to an abscess happening within the throat.
The ancients spoke of this incision of the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it; and this is their written account. ã€‹
In those who have a hernia, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided, as it will not be helpful; because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those who have a hot abscess in the mouth or throat or on both tonsils, when there is no pain in the throat, it is necessary to use the incision of the epiglottis to avoid death from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should cut the epiglottis under three or four circular ridges of the epiglottis with a small incision in width between two ridges so that the incision is in cartilage, not in bone: and this place is suitable for incision because it is free from flesh, and the blood vessels are far from it.
If the physician is timid, then he should stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him prick the skin until he reaches the throat; avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
Then let him cut the cartilage as we have described; and it is indicated over the incision of the throat from the mucus that comes out of it, with what is broken, and loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the hour approaches in which one fears suffocation, join the two lips of the skin wound, or just the skin alone without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that promote tissue growth until it heals.
Said Al-Zahrawi. The summary of the discussion we have presented is this: its explanation is only when they see that any one of these abscesses has filled the throat of the sick person and lifted him up to death, and he wishes to cut off his breath, they go to cut the epiglottis so that the sick person breathes over the place of the wound with a certain breath and is saved from death. And for this reason they commanded that the wound be left open until the wickedness of the illness is consumed, and its wickedness lasts three days or thereabouts; then they commanded to close the wound and heal it until it heals. ">
And that which I saw with my own eyes is that a maidservant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and cut with it a part of the lung's passageway.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her moaning as if she were dying.
Therefore, I untied from the wound, and I found blood that had come out of the small wound. So I became certain that she herself had not cut a vein or artery; and air was coming out of the wound.  \\
So I hastened and sutured the wound and treated it until it was healed; and nothing else happened to the maidservant except for hoarseness of voice, and after a few days she returned to her usual state.
Therefore I say that in cutting the epiglottis there is no danger.
Â¶ On the incision of a neck hernia.
Chapter 44
This swelling called a neck hernia is a large swelling the color of flesh, and it occurs frequently in women.
And there are two types: either it is natural or it is acquired.
In the natural type, certainly there is no intelligence.
The acquired type, however, occurs in two ways; one of which is similar to fatty knots.  /// And the other type is similar to the swelling that results from the knotting of an artery.  /// And there is danger in it, and one should not presume to cut into it with an iron except when it is small; and you should test it and examine it with a probe. If you find it similar to a fatty knot and not attached to any veins, then cut into it as one cuts a knot; and extract it with what it contains from the sac, if it is in a sac; and if not, then examine its entirety. Then care for the place with what is necessary from the treatment until it heals.
Chapter 45.
Of the section on swollen lumps.
There are many kinds of swollen lumps, and I have already mentioned all their types in the division; and it is necessary that I describe here the difference between a swollen lump and an abscess, since there is doubt about it.
I say therefore that when it is an abscess, there is heat, fever, and severe pain until the bubbling of the superfluity settles down and the putrefaction is completed; then indeed the fever and heat subside.
But when it is a swollen lump, there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain, and a kistis called sifachin contains them, which is their proper sac, and it is according to the color of the body; and its beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a watermelon, larger or smaller. ï¸Ž
And they are of two types: either fatty, or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a swollen lump, you should examine it and investigate it first with an instrument called Intromissum, according to its form which will be shown in the chapter that follows this one.
And the way of investigating all swollen lumps and tumors is to take this instrument and insert it into the moistest part that you find in the swelling; and you should turn your fingers with it little by little, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then you should place your hand according to the size of the swelling.
Then remove the Intromissum and examine what comes out in its wake.
If therefore a running moisture comes out, whatever its color may be, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I said in other swellings. ï¸Ž ï¸Ž
And if the moisture does not exit the wound after the insertion, then you should know that it is fatty: therefore, cut above it with a cross-shaped incision as I have taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and strip away the skin from all sides easily; and preserve kistin over it, if you can, so that you may extract it healthy with a knot.
But if the kist [cyst] bursts during the operation and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if any part of it, whether small or large, remains, the swelling will return to a great extent.
But if you are overcome and some part of it remains; then fill the wound at its conclusion with certain sharp corrosive powders; and bind the wound, and place a hot poultice over it, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Therefore, if it is a large swelling, then either separate its two lips, and treat it with what makes it heal.
But if the vein is contrary to you, either pulsating or non-pulsating; then hurry and fill the place with ground zegh [a type of plant], and leave it bound for two days, or three, until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of the blood ceases; then return to cutting what remains of the swelling.
Chapter 46.
Â¶ On external instruments for cutting and piercing.
From these are the forms of Intromission, and they are of three types.
Of these there is a large one, and of these there is a medium one, and of these there is a small one.
They should be made from square-ended iron files, so that they may hasten entry into the cyst.
And these are the forms of Surgeries, that is, incisions; and one of them is called beriÅ›, that is, incision; and there are three types.
For among them there is the large, the middle, and the small.
They are suitable for investigating through them boils, excretions, fistulas, and abscesses that are between the bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and gentle, like large needles with which upholstery is sewn, that is, from brass or iron or silver.
And the best of them are those made of brass.
And surgeries are also made of black lead, which are suitable for examining through them fistulas that have hollows, so that they may be examined with their gentleness and those hollows. ã€‹
And again, there are three types. For from them there are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the width of the fistula and its narrowness.
The form of incisions, and they are of many types.
For from them there are simple ones, that is, those which have one curve; and they are of three types as you see: large, medium, and small.
And from them are blind incisions, and they are of three types.
And from them are incisions having two curves, and they are of three types; and from them are incisions having three curves, and they are of three types; and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels with which nodes and boils are cut and scraped; and they are of three types, for from them there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
Their ends with which the section is made are sharp; their other ends are not sharp. And they are not placed thus except so that through them help may be given in the scraping of nodes because of the fear of cutting a vein or nerve, and so that the sick person may be healed through them, and may find temporary relief from the burning which is found in the scraping of the boil. ã€‹ ã€‹
They are made of air and resemble a ray with which alcohol is made. At the end of its broad tip there are hidden punctual points; through these it runs to the inside or the outside as you wish, just as you see.
The form of the hidden Spatumilium between the fingers in the perforation of the aposteme, and the weak do not perceive them; they are of three kinds: large, medium, and small.
The shapes of the Ventosarum with which the flow of blood is cut off; they are of three kinds: large, medium, and small.
They are made of air or citron wood, round and somewhat long, as you see, and should be very fine.
And it is necessary for you to have these large and small shapes of the Ventosarum, so that you may quickly cut off blood through them in an emergency when there is no medicine present.
However, do not use them to cut off blood in every part of the body, but only in fleshy places, such as the thighs of the leg and hip, the upper arms, breasts, abdomen, groin, and similar fleshy parts that are moist.
And sometimes from them are made other small instruments similar to the rinds of figs, according to their shape; with these blood is cut off when it flows from a place of venesection or from an incision of a vein or artery; and sometimes they are made round in this form.
Chapter 47.
Â¶ On the treatment of the breasts of humans which resemble the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of certain people swell at the end of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted during sleep), until they resemble the breasts of women; and they remain hideously inflamed.  />
He who dislikes it should cut above the breasts with a crescent-shaped incision according to this diagram from line b to line g. Then remove all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with an incarnative medicine. Then join the two lips of the wound together with a stitch and care for it until it heals.
But if the breast descends lower and softens due to its size, as happens with women: then one should make two crescent-shaped incisions on both of its upper sides, each joined with the other at both ends until a larger line encloses a smaller one, according to this diagram from line b to line g. Then remove the skin that is between the two lips, and take away the fat, and do as we said about the stitching and powders, which are necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision as you should due to the patient's restlessness or heavy bleeding: then you should fill the wound with cotton wool soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it has dissolved whatever fat remains. Then care for him until he heals.
Â¶ On the piercing of an abscess that occurs under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses that occur under the nipple are of the hard species of strophulae, which contain a bladder, and there are those among them which contain liquids.
Therefore, those which contain liquids should be cut with a crescent-shaped incision according to this form, from line b to line g. And when all that is in the abscess has been evacuated through pus, then fill it with old cotton wool and leave it until another day.
Then remove the bandage and clean with a suitable ointment until it heals.
If the abscess is of the kind called strophular nodes, then cut it open as we said in the section on equal cuts for strophular nodes.
If, however, the wound extends so that it discharges pus, then apply a cautery to it, as we said in what was mentioned before.
Chapter XLIX.
Â¶ About the incision of an abscess which occurs because of an artery and a vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it heals, then frequently an abscess occurs from there; and similarly this happens with a vein, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is recognized that an abscess and swelling are because of an artery and a vein are: because, when the abscess is because of an artery, it is long and deep in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a sound.  />
And that which is because of a vein is round and evident on the body.
And an incision over this abscess is dangerous, especially if it is under the armpits, groins, neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very great. Therefore, its treatment with a knife should be avoided, and those that are also in the limbs or head, their treatment should be avoided as well.
Therefore, for those caused by swelling of an artery opening, seek the cut along the length of the skin. Then open the cut with forceps, then the artery; and free it from its coatings until it is exposed.  />  />  />
Then insert a needle under it and make it pass to the other side; and bind the artery with double thread in two places, as I taught you in the removal of the two temporal arteries.  />  />
Then pierce the place that is between the two ligaments with a lancet, until all the blood comes out of it and the abscess is dissolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off; afterwards, treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If the abscess is due to a cut vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much of the abscess with the skin as possible. Then insert a needle under the place you held with your hand and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it until you extract it from the other side. Then bind the abscess with a good ligature, as I have told you for the ligature of the grape, on both sides with the two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread might be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess near the first needle's incision; press and bind your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in half until what is inside comes out; then cut away the excess skin and leave whatever has been bound from it.
Then place upon it a little piece of wool that you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply treatment with poultices and ointments until it heals.
Chapter L.
Â¶ About the abscess that occurs from a twisting of the nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too does an abscess occur in the nerves; this happens to them from blows or from excessive labor and similar things, and it occurs most often in the joints of the fingers or toes, and in any place where there is movement of the joints. And this is a grave abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many states without pain; but when squeezed firmly, the patient feels a sensation like numbness in it.
And there is no abscess deep within the body, but rather it is under the skin, and the entire thing can be moved in any direction; it does not go to the front or back towards the anterior or posterior parts.
Therefore, if something of this kind is in a joint, it should not be approached with iron. For perhaps contraction might occur.
As for what is of this kind in the head or forehead, incise the skin with a scalpel. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps and cut it off at the root.
If it is large, then hang it with a hook and scrape it; then remove it, and close the wound with stitches: and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LII.
Â¶ On the incision of an abscess that occurs in the belly.
There are certain people who often have lumps in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies, which are called fungalia due to their similarity to mushrooms. â€Œ â€Œ
Whose roots are fine, and whose heads are thick, and their lips are inverted. Some of them are small; others become very large.
I myself have seen a man who had two such lumps in his belly, which were more like mushrooms than anything else; there was no difference between them, they were white and with fine roots, their lips were inverted, and pus always flowed from them. â€Œ
I cut them open and found that one weighed about eight and a half ounces, the other about six ounces. And the operation for cutting into them is this: If the patient is humid and the color of the lump is white, watery, with fine roots, then cut it with a broad scalpel, and let cauteries be at hand in the fire.
Often much blood is expelled during its incision; therefore hasten if you are overcome by the blood and then cauterize it.
And if you see the patient weak, fearful and timid of the incision with iron, then take a thread well-made from lead, and string it through the athelul; its method is as we have said, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the constriction of the lead. Do not therefore cease tying the lead whenever it is impressed upon the thelil, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the thelil is of a thick root, then there is something fearful and terrifying in its incision, especially what is in the abdomen.
However, it is possible that you may incise half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then take care of it until the wound heals.
And beware of presuming to incise a thelil that is of a dark color, insensitive, and foul in appearance; for it is a cancerous abscess. And I will deal with the topic of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Â¶ On treating a protruding umbilicus.
An umbilical hernia can occur for many reasons. Or from a tearing of the diaphragm above the abdomen; and either an altub or intestine comes out of it, as happens in other cases of rupture. âž¤ Or from blood flowing from a vein or an artery, as was previously stated. Or from constipation constrained within it.
If however it is due to a tearing of the diaphragm and the exiting of intestines; then its color is similar to that of the body; and it is soft, without pain; and it appears in various places. âž¤ âž¤ And if it is due to the prolapse of the intestine, then its condition, as we have narrated, is of a more intense variety. So when you press it with your fingers, it is hidden; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it is greatly magnified at the entrance to the bath, and it is very troublesome.
But if it is due to moisture, it is gentle; then when you press it with your hand, it does not become hidden, nor constrained, nor increased.
But if it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears, turning black at the clavicle.
But if it is due to dead flesh, then the abscess is heavy, thick, hard, and it settles into one location.
But if it is due to flatulence, then its touch is gentle.
And the operation in it is such that you should then consider whether the swelling of the navel is due to blood from an artery or vein, or flatulence; then you must abstain from its treatment. For there is fear and danger in it, as I taught you in the chapter where I spoke about abscesses due to an artery or vein.
But if the swelling of the navel is due to the intestine or bladder, then you must advise the patient to hold his breath and stand upright and extended. Then mark with cautery the entire area around the navel. After that, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then surround the area around the navel with a wide bandage over the place that you marked with cautery. After that, extend the middle of the abscess upward with a large hook.  \\ Then bind it tightly around its circumference with thread or strong silk cord, and let the knot be an Alexandrian knot.
Then open the middle of the swelling extended above the ligament. If therefore you find that the intestine has already been caught by the ligament, then extract the hernia, and introduce your index finger into it, and push the intestine back towards the interior of the abdomen; and if you find the cecum, then take hold of it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
But if it is the opposite for you, that is, if it is an artery or vein, then encircle it much [with thread]. Then return to your work. And take two needles and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circle which you have made around the swelling in the manner of a cross, causing them to penetrate.
Then tie the swelling in four places above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the extended flesh has rotted and falls away by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten.
Then treat him with the necessary ointments until he is healed.
But if the swelling of the navel is from soft flesh or from moisture, then you should hollow out the swelling as we have said. And extract the blood, or the moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat him with that which will consolidate the place.
Chapter Fifty-Three
About the treatment of Cancers.
We have already spoken in the division of the kinds of Cancer, and how is the way to its treatment with medicines; and one must guard against its ulcerating with the iron during its treatment: and we have said that if a Cancer has been generated in the womb, one must take care in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes a Cancer is in a place where it is possible for it to be eradicated; such as a Cancer which is in the breasts or in the thigh, and similar places on the body, especially when it is small and just beginning.
When it is old and large, you should not approach it.
For I have not been able to heal anyone with it; nor did I see anyone before me who reached that point.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is such that you should precede and dissolve the patient from black bile often. Then perform a phlebotomy on him if you find clear evidence of fullness.
Then prepare the patient with the preparation that is appropriate for the operation.
Then cast into the Cancer the curved hooks that are suitable for it. Then hollow it out completely with the skin from every side, according to your examination, until nothing remains of its roots; and let the blood flow, and do not cut it off quickly, but press on the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand or whatever possible instrument is there.
But if the flow of thick blood from the cutting of an artery or vein causes you difficulty, then cauterize the vein until the blood is cut off.  /> And it should not be done on a young person except for necessity.  /> And this is what we have written about it. Then care for him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
Â§ On the Treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter LIV. We have already explained in the division of the kinds of dropsy and how water accumulates; and the signs of every kind, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only the kind of ascites alone; and it does not approach the kind of tympanites with iron, nor the kind of hypoascites at all; for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this kind of dropsy tympanites with medicines, and your treatment did not help; then consider. For if the patient has already become weakened or there is another illness with him besides the dropsy, such as if he has a cough or loose bowels and similar to those; then beware lest you treat him with iron; for there is danger in that.
If however you see the patient with an abundance of vigor, and besides hydropsy alone there is no other illness with him, and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the manner of operating on him is for you to lift the patient standing between your hands, and an assistant behind him should press his belly with his hand, forcing water down to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a thorny sponge, in this shape. Let it be of two sharp ends, long, with a sharp tip like a thorny sponge. However, there should be something of shortness to it gradually, so that you do not penetrate with it during the operation all the way to the intestine, and damage it.
Then pay attention. For if the hydropsy is generated from the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend with a cut from the navel by three fingers, to the lower part of it directly above the chest.
However if the hydropsy is generated because of an illness of the liver, then your cut should be on the left from the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let there be a cut on the right side: and it is not necessary that there also be a cut on the side where the patient wishes to lie, so that the superfluities do not run to that weak place.
Then pierce the whole skin with the instrument. Then introduce into that cut, and lift your hand with the thorny sponge under the skin, as if you were scraping it off: and let the amount that is scraped be the size of a nail or about that.
Then pierce with the sifac, until the thorny sponge reaches the empty place: and it is a place of water. Then extract it, and introduce into the opening an instrument, the shape of which is this. And it is similar to a reed: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth and clean; at its lower part there is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side and one on the other side; and its tip is cut according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see; at its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately sinks onto the instrument; therefore evacuate half the amount of water in an hour.
Because if you evacuate more than is necessary from it, then perhaps the patient will die within the hour due to the loss of his vital spirit; or a fainting spell will occur which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the measure of its power, and according to what the condition of the patient indicates to you by the strength of his pulse, and by his good state.
Then remove the instrument, and keep the water.
This is because it is immediately retained due to the skin which holds closed the hole that is above the syphon; concerning which I have said that you should pierce it in this manner.
Then repeat the use of the instrument on the following day, if you see that the patient is able to tolerate it; and also evacuate a small amount of water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error; until there remains only a little water.
But if you are afraid for the patient and let out a large amount of water, then care for him with burial in hot sand, and much sweat in the bath and sun, and wait for him to be thirsty: and care for him with drying medicines until he is cured; and cauterize over the stomach, liver, and spleen after the evacuation of water, as I have said in what preceded.

Â¶ Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of their mistaken conditions.
Circumcision is not a dissolution of continuity, like other wounds; nevertheless, since it is our operation, by our will, and its use is proper for boys, it is necessary that we mark this operation with something better, and find an easier way leading to health.
I therefore say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books because they do not use it in their laws, and we have only learned about it through experience.
And this is because I have found that the majority of craftsmen and bloodletters use cleansing by cutting with a razor and with scissors; and they use cautery and ligation with thread, and incision with a nail.
And indeed I have already tested all these methods, and I have not found a better cleansing than with scissors and ligation with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes (i.e., is cut too deeply). Because the skin of the foreskin has two layers; therefore it may happen that the upper layer is cut and the lower layer remains intact; thus an incision is necessary to cut the other, which will cause pain. And with cleansing that is done by cautery, which is what shoemakers use, the incision of the tip of the male member is not secured because perhaps it may enter its opening.
But in cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron may slip and spoil your work; and indeed the child's skin is naturally short, and often they are born such that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen this.
But cleansing with scissors and ligation with thread has shown me to be a good experiment because the scissors are of an appropriate size for the incision; therefore, the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. ï¸ Therefore when you press with your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned and they cut at the same time according to one operation; and a similar-shaped compression is made, containing the skin of the penis from all sides, with which no error occurs at all.
And the method of operation is to cause the child, especially if he understands a little, to think that you are not doing anything except tying a string on his stick; and let him go until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be merry in every way possible for you to do so, according to his understanding. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, not sitting; and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the child does not see them at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand towards his stick and blow on the skin, and pull it up until the head of the stick comes out. Then cleanse it because filth accumulates in it.
Then bind the marked place with double thread. Afterwards, bind a little lower the second ligature. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature firmly, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and remove the head of the stick. Then allow the blood to flow slowly (for it is better) and place powder on top; and on the powder a cloth in which a cooked egg yolk has been spread with fresh good rose oil, shaken together; and leave it on him until another day; and take care of him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
The form of the scissors for cleansing is as follows:
The two ends should be blunt and should not be sharp; so that the nail-pin does not protrude from them; and let the length of the two sharpened parts be equal to the length of the two handles.
The mistake that occurs in cleansing is if perhaps the entire inner skin or a part of it escapes during the incision.

"Concerning the removal of retained urine from the bladder.

Then gradually extend the thread with the wool. For urine will follow the wool.
Then draw it out and let the urine flow through its natural pathway: and repeat with the syringe, and do not cease to do this until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Â¶ On the Method of Enema for the Bladder using a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When there occurs an ulcer in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or pus thickens in it, and it is necessary to instill waters and medicines into it: then it should be done with an instrument called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.  /> Â¶ De forma Clepshydrae.
And the hollow place, which contains that which propels, should be according to its size without addition, until when it is drawn with something, water is drawn; and when it is propelled, it expels with it to a distance, just as the clepsydra does, by which naphtha, that is, Greek fire, is projected in the battles of Mars.
Therefore, when you wish to throw the liquids into a bladder, introduce the end of the clepsydra into the liquid and draw with it that which propels upward, for then the liquid is drawn into the hollow of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its end into a tube, as we have described with the syringe. Then propel the liquid with that which propels, for then the liquid immediately reaches the bladder so that the patient feels it.
The form of a fine enema to irrigate the bladder.
It should be made of silver or copper: whose head is similar to a small alchemist's still and beneath it there is a pouch into which the string falls.  (Or: ... whose head is shaped like a small alembic, and below it there is a pouch into which the string descends.)
Then take the bladder of a ram and place it in that liquid with which you wish to irrigate the bladder. Then tie it strongly between two pouches with a double thread, and heat that liquid slightly over the fire.  (Or: ... between two bags with a strong double thread, and heat that liquid for a little while on the fire.)
Then introduce the end of the enema into a tube; afterward, place your hand on the bladder with the liquid until you feel it tight enough, so that the patient feels that this liquid has now reached the bladder.
If however you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle out of it and pierce it from all its circumference. Then introduce a thread fortunately in those openings, as they roll around. Then make a circle like an embroiderer does: after you have placed upon it what you wish from the moist substances, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it onto the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder for emptying with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60
Â§ About extracting a stone.
I have already spoken in the division of the types of stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I stated the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and all the signs of that; and I spoke about the stone on which manual operation and incision are effective: it is properly a stone generated in the bladder, and one that becomes fixed in the shaft; and I am explaining this with clear exposition and abbreviation.
I say therefore, that a stone generated in the bladder occurs most often in boys.
And its signs are that the urine exits from the bladder like water in its fineness; and there appears white sand in it; and the patient rubs his shaft, and struggles with it, and often it hangs down. Then he erects [his penis], and exits with it the anus in many of them.
And treatment for boys is easy, until they reach fourteen years [of age]. And it is difficult in the elderly. But in young men it is moderate, in that it is between [those two]: and for someone whose stone is larger, the treatment is easier; but if small, then its care is the opposite. â€º
Therefore, when we have come to treatment, it is first necessary to purify the patient with an enema, which removes all the excrement that is in his intestines; for this itself sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone upon investigation.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet, and shaken, and moved towards the lower part: so that the stone may descend to the depth of the bladder, or jump from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his knees, so that the entire bladder may become sloping downwards towards the lower part.
Then examine him, and touch him from the outside.
If therefore you feel a stone in a space, then hurry immediately with an incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then you must clean the index finger with oil of the left hand, if the patient is a boy; or the middle finger, if he is a grown man; and insert it into his anus, and search for the stone, until it stands firm under your finger: and gradually turn him towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and push it towards the exterior against the place where you wish to make an incision; and command one assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another assistant to extend the testicles with his right hand above: and with his other hand, to remove the skin which is under the testicles from the place in which is the incision.
Then insert a cutting spatula, whose form is this. And find it between the anus and testicles, not in the middle but to the left of the natis; and let the incision be upon the stone itself, and your finger be in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that the external part of the incision may be wide, and the internal part narrow, according to the size which permits the egression of the stone from it not greater.  />
For perhaps the finger that is in the anus presses upon the stone at the incision, and it comes out without difficulty.
And know that from the Stone is which has angles and edges, why its exit is difficult because of it.
And it is smooth, like a hazelnut, and round; and its exit becomes easy.
Therefore, to the one which has angles and edges, add into the cleft.
If it does not exit thus, then you must contrive a device on top of it; or tie a silk thread neatly around it, whose end is like the moon, which ties above the stone and does not escape from it; or that you insert underneath a fine instrument with a curved tip.
But if you cannot do so on top of it, then slightly enlarge the opening. If something overcomes you from the blood, cut it with a thread.
But if there is more than one stone, then first drive the largest to the mouth of the bladder. Then cut into it. After that, drive the smaller one after it; and do likewise if there are more than two.
If it is very large, it is a mistake to cut into it with a large incision. Because two things happen to the patient: either he dies, or a continuous dribble of urine happens; therefore, because the place does not heal at all.
Truly administer its expulsion until it exits; or contrive devices in its fragmentation with forceps until you draw it out piece by piece.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense and aloe and dragon's blood; and bind it up, and place on top of it a cloth soaked in oil and wine or rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may subside.
Then make the patient lie on their back with their head elevated, and do not remove the bandage until the third day. When you have loosened it, drench the place with water and much oil.
Then treat him with dyafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until he is cured.
However, if an inflamed abscess with burn-like symptoms occurs, such as when blood coagulates in the bladder and blocks the flow of urine, it is recognized by the presence of blood in the urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply all suitable treatments for its healing until it is cured.
And during the entire course of treatment, it is necessary to bind together both thighs and buttocks so that the medicines placed on the area remain in place.
If the stone is small and lodged in the meat of the penis and blocks the flow of urine, then treat him with that which I have mentioned before you reach the point of incision. For often you will be spared from incision by this treatment, for I have already tested it.
And it is necessary to take a perforator made of steel, the shape of which is as follows: triangular tip, sharp, and fixed in wood.
Then take a thread and tie it to the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the perforating iron into the penis with ease until the perforator reaches the stone itself; and gradually turn the perforator by hand in the stone, attempting to pierce it through until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For immediately the urine is released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone from the outer part of the rod. For it is broken and exits with the urine, and the patient is healed.
If this treatment is not prepared for you because of something that prevents it, then tie a thread below the stone and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the rod itself between the two ties. Then extract it. After that, loosen the tie and cleanse the clotted blood that forms at the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie the thread below the stone except so that it does not return to the bladder; and another tie above except that when the thread is loosened after the exit of the stone, the skin may return to its place and cover the wound. And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the thread above, to lift the skin upward so that it may return at your completion and cover the wound as we have said.

Chapter Sixty-Two.
Â¶ On incision for a hernia containing fluid.
A watery hernia is nothing but a collection of moisture in the white sac that lies under the skin and contains the testicle, which is called the scrotum.
And sometimes it occurs in the proper pouch that nature has prepared for it within the testicle, which is another sac and lies between the white scrotum that we mentioned and the skin of the testicle; this happens only rarely. Â• If you wish to operate on a watery hernia, do not cut near the skin or the place where the two sacs meet, but rather in the center of the lower part of the scrotum, cutting it completely through.
The cause of this hernia is indeed the weakness that affects the testicles, which is why this material flows into them. And sometimes it happens from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For its color is either inclined towards yellowish, or it is a reddish blood-color, or dark and feculent, or watery white; the last of these is the most common. Â• The operation for this kind of hernia is the same as that which we have described for the removal of stones in men.
And the signs by which one knows where the collection of water is located are these: "For if it is in the white scrotum that we have mentioned, then the abscess is round in length, a little like the shape of an egg; and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it on all sides.
"And if there is moisture in its own covering, then the abscess is round like part of an egg, and because of this, a person might think it is another egg.
"And if there is moisture between the skin of the testicles and the white scrotum, then it falls under sensation.
"When you wish to know the color of the moisture, then test the color of the moisture in the abscess with the previously mentioned square instrument. "According to what emerges as a trace of that which was inserted, judge that which is between them.
"Therefore, when we come to the cure with iron, it is necessary for you to instruct the patient that they should be bled if possible, and see his body full.
"Then let him lie on his back over something a little high, and place many cloths under him.

"Then take a broad spoon and cut the skin of the testicles lengthwise in the middle, almost to the pubic hair; and let the incision be straight along the equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white scrotum containing it; and remove it and observe, or be careful not to cut it: and let your excision by which you remove it, be from the side where it hangs more with the egg. And examine it carefully with the excision according to what is possible for you.
Then perforate the sponge full of water with a wide perforation, and remove all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the section with hooks, and extend the sponge to above, and do not touch the skin enclosing the testicles: and cut the sponge however it is possible for you to cut it, either all at once or in pieces, especially its thin side. For if you do not carefully examine, there will be no certainty that the water does not return upon cutting it.

But if the water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches; cut off the other part just as you did to the first one equally.

Chapter 69.  />  />
Â¶ About castration.
You should know that castration is excommunicated according to our law: and because of this it would be permitted for me not to mention it in my book; and there is no need to mention it except for two reasons.
One of these is so that it may be known by the physician when he is asked about it, and so that he may know how to treat the one on whom it has occurred.
And the other reason is that we often need the castration of certain animals for our benefit through it, such as rams, asses, cats, and similar creatures.
I say therefore, that castration is done in two ways. Either by attrition. Or by incision and cutting.
In that which is done by attrition, the way of the operation is this: we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles become softened and relaxed and hang down; then you grind them with your hand until they dissolve and do not appear to touch.
But in the castration by incision and cutting, it is necessary for you to hold the animal and press its testicular skin with your left hand.
Then bind the suspensory ligament, and cut above both testicles with a single incision until two testicles protrude; then cut them after you have removed the skin from each, and do not leave anything on them except for a thin layer of tissue that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, and so the animal desires copulation.
Then care for the wound until it heals.

Then let it be stretched equally and not only straight but also moved in every direction to ease its removal, as is done with a tooth during extraction.
And it should be so that what is between them softens the extension. If a part is retained from it, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil and insert them into that part to rotate and remove what is retained.
If a part of the fetus comes out, then move the hooks to another place that is higher; do this until the entire fetus comes out.
If a hand comes out before the others and it is not possible to return it because of its swelling; then a cloth should be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until the entire hand comes out; cut it either at the joint of the foot or at the elbow; and thus you should do for the other hand and both feet.
If however the fetus' head is large or becomes swollen at birth, or if there is water gathered on its head; then a thorny spatula should be inserted between the fingers and used to pierce the head and remove the water; or it should be ruptured with an instrument called Almisdac. The shape of which will come with the shapes of the other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus is by nature of large head, then one should cut with a trepan and extract it with the instrument called misdach, as we have said. Then draw out the bones with forceps.
If the head comes out and becomes swollen at the collarbone; then pierce it until the fluid from the chest is released.
But if it does not become complicated nor submit to birth; then cut the head piece by piece as is possible for you.
If the swelling is in the lower abdomen and it is accompanied by dropsy, you should pierce it until the moisture runs out.
If however the fetus is on its feet, then its delivery is easy and its direction toward the mouth of the womb is gentle.
But if it is constricted in the abdomen or chest, then you should extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and open the abdomen or chest until what is inside flows out.
But if the other limbs have been delivered and only the head remains, then the midwife should insert her left hand. Therefore, if the mouth of the womb is open, let her insert her hand deep into the womb and search for the head with it, and draw it to the mouth of the womb with her fingers; then let her insert into it one hook or two hooks, and draw it out with them.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted because of a hot swelling that has befallen her, then it is not fitting to labor over it; rather, it is fitting at that time to apply emollients and fatty substances, and administer poultices, and sit in waters that soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, it may turn; then let what we said be administered for a living fetus be given.
If that is not possible, then cut it piece by piece and extract it.
And you must not leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all.
Therefore, when you have completed your work, then administer what we said for hot swellings that occur in the womb.
But if there is a flow of blood, then let her sit in styptic waters and administer the rest of the treatment.
And indeed I have seen a woman whose womb contained a dead fetus; then she became pregnant with another, and afterward that fetus also died. And so after a long time an abscess occurred on her navel, and it swelled until it burst; and she began to discharge pus.
Therefore I was called for her treatment, and I treated her for a long time; yet the wound did not heal.
And so I placed on it certain strong drawing plasters; and a mouth came out of that place. Then after several days another mouth emerged from there. Therefore I was amazed by this, since there is no mouth in the womb.
And so I thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.  \\
And so I examined the wound and extracted many bones while the woman was in good health; and now she has lived in this way for a long time, and she emits little pus from that place.
And I would not have noted this strange occurrence here unless there is knowledge and help in it for what a skilled doctor does with his hand in treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Â¶ On the Forms of Necessary Instruments for Fetal Extraction.
The shape of the loop by which the womb is opened.
Here is the form of the instrument called the stricture, which is used to equalize the lips; it has two rollers equally placed at the ends of two wooden pieces. However, this roller must be finer than the stricture; and it is made either from ebony or boxwood.  />   \\
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers; and the edge of each is about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood there are two attachments made from the same type of wood, which have already been firmly attached to both, whose length is more than a half-palm; and the width of each is about two fingers, or slightly more.
And these two instruments are inserted into the womb's mouth so that it may be opened when you turn two loops.
Another form similar to that one, but finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood, according to the shape of forceps. However, at its end there should be two instruments, as you see here above: and the length of each instrument is like a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore, when you wish to open the womb with it, make the woman sit on a bed with her feet hanging down and her legs spread apart. Then insert these two connected instruments into the womb, and you are holding the end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount that you wish for the opening of the womb's mouth until it does what the midwife wants.
The shape of another loop, which the ancients called the shape of the pusher, by which the fetus is pushed out.
The shape of Almisdagh, by which a large head is crushed, and it resembles forceps with teeth at the end, as you see. xspace
And sometimes it is made elongated, like forceps according to this shape, as you see, with teeth like a saw, by which it cuts and crushes.
The shape of the one-spined hook. xspace xspace
The shape of the two-spined hook.
Form with two ends for cutting off the head.
Form of two broad spatulas.
All these instruments are used, and the more their kinds are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the swifter he is at his task, and the greater he stands among men because of his skill.
Therefore, do not disdain any of these instruments so that it may be prepared by you. Their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Â¶ On the Extraction of Secundina.
When the Secundina is retained during childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the patient to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with you: and press your hand on her mouth and nose. If it therefore comes out through this [method], that is good.
If not: then take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid; and put in it opening herbs of the womb, such as calamentum, rue, dill, chamomile, saffron, cassia wood, and centaury; take all these herbs or some of them, and steep them with water and place [them] on the fire.
Then place a reed funnel on the hole of the pot lid, and its other end in the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. Â• For indeed the Secundina will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not come out, then instruct the midwife to submerge her hand in sesame oil or in thickened althea [mallow]. Then let her insert [her hand] into the vulva and seek with it the Secundina. "When he finds it, then let him take it and slowly extend it.
"If however it is attached deep within the womb, then he should insert his hand as we have described until he finds the placenta and gradually extracts it straight away so that the womb does not collapse due to excessive force. Rather, it should be turned gently to the right and left, and then pulled according to the amount of traction needed. "For at that point, she will comply, be saved, and escape from the obstruction.
"But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described, care for her with sneezing and with a decoction made from herbs.
"But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself too much during the process of extraction.
"Truly, whatever comes out at the mouth of the womb from her, you should tie to the woman's thigh. "Then enema her with the four-ingredient ointment. "For that will cause it to putrefy after a few days and it will dissolve and come out.
"However, when it putrefies, an evil smell rises to the stomach and head, harming the sick person. "Therefore, it is necessary that suitable fumigations be administered for that.
"Some of the ancients have already experienced the usefulness of fumigation with cress and dried figs."
"Description of the instrument used to fumigate a woman during retained placenta or menses.
"Let it be made from a glass similar to an alcama, or from wax. The wide end should be placed over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed on coals. Let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed, and then repeat with another.
Chapter 79.
On the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with unperforated anuses, which have already been blocked by a thin film. Therefore, the midwife should pierce that thin film with her finger; and if she cannot, she should pierce it with a sharp spatula, taking care not to touch the scrotum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on top of it. Then care for him with ointments until he is healed.
But if you fear that it will become blocked again, then place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the boy wants to defecate.
And now an obstruction of the anus can also occur due to the healing of a wound or an abscess. ã€‹ Therefore, that healing should be cut away, and then it should be treated with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter 80.
Â¶ On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are knots and swellings that happen near the anus outside or in the space of one fingerbreadth; and there is one fistula or more.
Therefore, when that knot is cut open, it opens and from it flows a watery white fluid or thin pus.
And sometimes there are fistulas that penetrate from these. Therefore, the penetrating ones are recognized by what comes out during defecation and flatulence when the sick person uses defecation; and perhaps a worm comes out of them. ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹
And there are fistulas among these when they penetrate in the space as far as the bladder and as far as the opening of the penis. ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ And there are some that penetrate as far as the hip joint and as far as the tailbone. ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹
When it is recognized that the fistula reaches to the anus, whether penetrating or not, do this: insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe of iron or steel into the fistula if there is no twisting.
If there is a twisting, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or a horsehair until you feel the probe or hair with your finger.
If you do not feel it completely and nothing comes out of the opening, neither wind nor worm as I said, then know that it is not penetrating; hasten to treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder or the opening of the urine, then its sign is the issuing of urine from it and its prevention so that the place may be consolidated with medicines.
However, if it is penetrating to the joint of the thigh or the hip socket, then its sign is that a probe reaches there if there is no twisting up to the opening; and the discovery of pain around the part, and that pus always comes out of it; and that treatment in it does not avail nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their treatment is labor and vanity for him who wastes his time on them.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, a cure is hoped for with a saw as I narrate to you and have already proved: and it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back; and raise his legs above his hips and his hips be bent toward his belly. Then insert a probe of lead or iron if there is twisting in the fistula until you know where the probe has reached.
If however the patient feels it around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
Therefore, if you feel on your finger the probe has passed through itself, apart from what you feel between your finger and the probe or flesh: then you should certainly know that it is penetrating. So do not struggle with it, for there is no cure for it, as we have said.
They have said, however, that some people are cured when the flesh is cauterized and the medication by which they hope for improvement is to ignite a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as was previously described, and insert it burning into the fistula until it reaches the anus. Then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those added fleshy bits have been burned away, similar to a bird's feather quill.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter until the burned flesh comes out; then treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth and consolidation.
If it is healed, good.
And if not, then it will never be cured by any other treatment.
And if the probe comes out and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or probe between them, and you see that the fistula follows the surface of the skin: then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and go cutting with the probe that is in the fistula until the cutting reaches where the end of the probe is, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then clean away those fleshy bits that are similar to a bird's feather quill, so that nothing of them remains.
But if the bleeding overcomes you and loosens between you and your work so that you can cut those fleshy bits: then it is better what happens, and more rapid healing, to cauterize with fire or sharp medication.
Because the cautery, combined with fire, possesses beneficial properties, it adds to the burning of those tissues and the cutting off of blood, as well as the drying out of moisture.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter or pounded cabbage leaves until the place discharges pus and the burned flesh comes out with the pus.
Then treat the wound with healing ointments that produce hard flesh; and these are medications that contain astringents and drying agents.
For when they are restored, then it is healed; and do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula has already reached the depth of the rectum and extends from an excess of bodily fluid, then insert your finger into the rectum and examine with it.
Therefore, if you feel a band or membrane of silk or flesh between yourself and it, and it is close to perforation, then there is no operation on it except by the way of trust and hope.
This is because one of three methods is applied to it: either you cauterize it as we said, or you cut it out until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert linen cloths and nearby treatment. And do not proceed too much with the cutting so that you do not cut the ligament that holds the rectum and cause the unfortunate outcome of incontinence without intention. Then treat it as we have said.
For perhaps it will heal; or you may lengthen the membrane itself and cleanse it after cutting it nearly to the rectum as we said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse those pieces of flesh that are in the incision as far as you can with probing.
Then strive to restore the entire wound with the incision and the upper orifice where it is healed; and let the fistula remain open in the interior part of the rectum, and it will be less harmful to the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is treated in this manner: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with skin and edge of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for embroidering, that is, preparing hides, in this manner. And insert into it a thread wound from five threads, and about that length. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its depth.
But if it is penetrating the edge of the anus from within near to it, then withdraw the thread from that opening; in such a way that you insert your finger into the anus and draw out the end of the thread, and bring together both ends at once; and tie each one, and leave it for one day or two days. And however much the thread cuts into the flesh, tie it well, until the flesh between the two ends of the thread is cut and falls away; then treat the wound until it heals and cures.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate by whatever means you prepare for yourself, unless it is very deep. For you do not have the ability to do this for the sake of the fleshy part, so that you do not cut into it.
Then treat it as we have said until it heals.
The form of a spiky spatula by which the fistula is probed, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter 81
Â¶ On the suture of hemorrhoids from which blood flows after their incision, and on the treatment of fissures.
There are two types of hemorrhoids. Or indeed, they are within the anus, similar to bladders of coal or plums; or as if they were themselves the seeds of grapes. And from them are small ones and large ones, and blood always flows from them, and it is one, and they are many. And they are outside the anus and at its ends.
Nevertheless, those which are outside the anus are for the most part of little moisture, from which runs yellow water or a small amount of blood with persistent flow, and they correspond in color to the body.
And its treatment which is within the anus is to advise the sick person to take a seat and to squeeze until the anus comes out and reveals the hemorrhoids to you. Therefore, hang them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
But if hooks do not hold them due to their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or a piece of coarse woolen cloth and draw them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision sprinkle on them some warm powders so that they serve as a caustic. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they heal.
But if the anus does not comply with coming out, then give the sick person a clyster in which there is a small amount of biting (irritant), so that it washes away what is in the anus and quickly complies with coming out when the patient squeezes.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their treatment is light and easy; and it is as if you take them with your fingernail or take them with a hook and cut them. Then care for them with what we have said until they heal.
And he who abhors the cutting with iron, then it is necessary for you to apply their stitches in this manner. Take thread wound around and push it through a needle. Then draw out the thread to the top and make it penetrate with the needle into its base from the other side; and wind the two ends of the thread beneath the point of the needle, so that they are lateral; and tighten the thread tightly. Then tie off the thread and withdraw the needle.
Do this with all the threads, but leave one out; nor draw it tight, so that excess blood may run from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cedar oil, and place it over the anus; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave him until he passes it.
When the threads fall out, care for them with ointments and other treatment until they heal.
Â¶ On the Treatment of Fissures. For often they happen from the drying out of feces and constriction of nature. â€º
When they become chronic and the medicine is ineffective, then it is necessary for you to scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your nail until they are moistened and the top layer which prevents them from healing is removed. Then care for them until they heal as is necessary.
But if they do not heal, then repeat the scraping with greater force than before until they bleed much; then care for them until they heal. â€º â€¹
