Â¶ On the bleeding of veins.  />
There are thirty veins that run in the body and into which it is customary to bleed.
Of which there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins behind the ears, called the two herbs; and two arteries that appear in both temples; and two veins in both tear ducts of the eyes, which are called the watchers; and one prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and one vein at the tip of the nose. And two veins of Guidugi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
But the veins which are bled in the arms are five in each arm.
One of them is called the cephalic vein and it comes from the outer side; and the common people name it the head vein.  /> And the dark one. And it is the median vein and it is formed from a branch of the basilic vein and a branch of the cephalic vein; and the common people call it the body vein. And the basilic vein, which is situated on the inner side, and is also called the tickler. And the common people call it the belly vein. And the cord of the arm, which is situated over the axilla; and that is the one which is bled, and it appears clearly above the thumb.
And the little saver: and it is a vein between the ring finger and the little finger, and there are two branches of it.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins.  />  />  /> One of them is the one which is under the arch of the thumb on the outer side.  />  /> And the second one is the saphenous vein, and its location is near the heel on the inner side. And the nessian vein; and its place is near the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Â¶ About the bleeding of two veins behind the ears.
However, the bleeding of two veins that are behind the ears is beneficial for old coughs, and headaches, and sahafati, and long-standing bad sores on the head.
And the manner of bloodletting it is as I will tell you. And it is done by shaving the head of the patient; and his occipital region where the two veins are located should be rubbed with a rough cloth using good friction. Then the patient should tie their neck with a band, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in the two depressed areas of the head. Seek them therefore with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that spot with encaustic paint.
Then take the bloodletting knife, which is called the incisor. Then insert it under the vein into the skin, until the bloodletter reaches the mouth of the vein. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin using an incising cut. And let the length of the incision be two fingers' breadths joined together, or about that: and allow as much blood to flow out as you wish.
Then tie it with small bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And now at the beginning of the book there has been explained the cutting of them and their own cauterization.
The bloodletting of the two arteries in the temples is beneficial for chronic migraine; and for recurrent dizziness; and for persistent headache; and for the flow of acute discharges to the eyes. â– 
And the manner of bloodletting them is as I will tell you. Let the patient tighten their neck strongly, until the two veins become clearly visible to the touch; and let their pulse be felt under your finger. â–  â–  Then mark it with encaustic paint.
Then lift up the skin with your index finger above the vein and insert a lancet underneath it, and lift up the vein above. And cut it, as you did with the two other veins: and let the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the binding of the patient, and place your finger over the vein for one hour. Then put a cloth and bandages on it and bind it tightly above; and leave it until it heals.
And now the description of them, their cutting, and their extraction has been mentioned at the beginning of this book.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after cephalic phlebotomy for old facial diseases, and for migraine, and redness, and foul ulcers.
And its quality is as I tell you. Let the patient bind their neck with a band until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called a scarifier. Â¶
Place the projecting point of the scarifier on the vein and strike it with a comb or something similar, allowing the blood to flow according to your will. Then loosen the patient's binding and bind it until it heals.
Sometimes it is opened with a wide lancet. However, it should not be a sharp-pointed lancet like the others; rather, it should have a broad point. And cut the lancet according to the direction of the vein, because the mouth is nearby; and perhaps a thin lancet will break when it is there. Â¶ Â¶
The affliction of the two veins that are in the two tear ducts of both eyes is for eye disease from scabies and redness: it causes blindness in the eye and facial pain.
And the method of bleeding them is that the patient tightens his own neck with a cord. Then perform the bloodletting on these veins, and you should be standing over their head.
And let the bloodletting be done transversely along its length a little bit, with a small fleam, wide a little bit, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the fleam is too fine at the tip, it might break.
Then let as much blood flow out as you deem necessary. And place a bandage over them, and bind them for one night, and then remove the bindings.
The veins of the nose are beneficial for acute fever, severe headache, and facial pain, such as migraine and facial redness, especially when chronic.
And the method of bleeding them is that the patient tightens his own neck. xspace Then hold the patient's nose with your left hand, and take a fine, long fleam, and insert it into the middle of the lip between the two parts that divide the nose along its length. Because the vein is not visible or palpable there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately. xspace ething
And you must hold your hand with the fleam a little bit, and let as much blood flow out as necessary. xspace ething ething ething ething ething ething Then bandage it for one night. For it recovers quickly.
The two bloodlettings of Guy are beneficial for constriction of shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, melancholic diseases, impetigo, and bad ulcers, as well as for corrodents, that is, herpetic eczema.
And the method of these bloodlettings is for the patient to tighten both his veins under his collarbone with a ligature; and let the physician stand over the head of the patient, while the patient sits on a stool.
Then the phlebotomist should cut the vein for bloodletting slightly longer than usual. Then draw from the blood a measured amount, or as much as is necessary for that purpose. Then do the same with the other vein.
Afterward, release the ligature and tighten both veins moderately so that the patient is not overly constricted. And leave it thus until morning. For the wound will heal.
About venous bloodletting of Al-Ghafiqi. The bloodletting of the veins of Al-Ghafiqi is beneficial because it helps with a sore throat, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, and fissures of the lips, as well as bad ulcers that are also on the nose and its surroundings.
And the method of these bloodlettings is to have the patient sit before you and tie his neck with a cord. Then turn his lip and look at the two veins, one of which is on the right side of the lip and the other on the left, and you can identify each by the blackness of both. And this is true because around them are small, dark veins. Therefore, cut both with a single incision. (): 100)
If however there is doubt about which one it is and you do not know which one it is, then pay attention to the larger of them and the more obvious.
And do the same with the two veins that are in the upper lip, for this is the most common practice; and cut the two veins that are in the lower lip.
Â¶ On bloodletting: After the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein, the veins under the tongue should be bled. The advantage of the bloodletting of these two veins that are under the tongue is that the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for throat diseases and also for mouth disease and other oral diseases.
And the manner of bleeding them is to have the patient sit between your hands in front of the sun, and lift up his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side a vein, and on the other side another vein, and the color of both leans towards blackness.
Therefore, bleed both; and be careful not to finish bleeding them with your hand. For under these [veins] are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood from those arteries may occur.
And the three veins that are bled in the elbow, the bloodletting of these is commonly practiced on all people.
Their bloodletting is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle bloodletting needle, or with an olive wood needle that has been planed to sharpness; or by cutting with a surgical knife, which is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And of these there are broad and fine types according to the size and narrowness of the veins. The form of the myrtle bloodletting needle should be broad, as you see. And it is fitting for opening hollow, full, prominent, large veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomy is of lesser width and more delicate in its ends.
And it is fitting for phlebotomy of subtle veins, which contain thin citrine blood.
However, the basilica, which is one of these three veins for phlebotomy, is useful. Because it extracts blood from illnesses that are under the throat and neck, since it follows the chest and belly: and it is fitting for the phlebotomist to be cautious when he phlebotomizes it, because of fear of it, for under it there is an artery. If therefore he errs and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore it is necessary that he not phlebotomize her with the phlebotomy needle; rather let him cut with alnacil. Â•
If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then it is necessary to remove it and replace it with another one, or look for some of its branches; or in place of that vein, the vein of the arm. For it is clear, and cut it with a phlebotomy needle, as we said.
But if you want to phlebotomize that same one, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, to touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with cautery, afterward constrict the arm, and cut the vein with a concave section using the phlebotomy needle called alnacil, as we said. Â• And be careful that the incision does not fall into the space of the artery.
And when you see, as you tie the ligature, swelling in the place marked with cautery, then that swelling is of the artery; therefore, avoid it.
If you see blood spurt at the site of venipuncture, as in a child's urine, and it is fine and red; then know that it is from the artery. Then hasten, and place your finger on it for a long time, and afterwards remove your finger. Therefore, if the blood is cut (for it is often cut), then tie off the arm and leave it; and let the patient beware of moving it; and let him be in fear of it.
And do not move it for several days until it heals.
But if the blood does not spurt and it overcomes you; and you do not have a remedy at hand, then cut the artery, if it appears to you.  â€‹ For its extremities contract, and the blood spurts out.
Or take the bark of a fig tree and pound it; take half of it, and tie it over the vein with a suitable bandage, along with a thread and cotton wool until another day.
Then if the blood spurts out, that is good. And if it does not spurt out, then treat him as described above for the dust that causes blood to flow; and its bleeding is not difficult in many conditions because of the small wound and the possibility of tying off from the arm.
Venipuncture of the dark vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; for this reason, it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.  â€‹
And it is suitable for the person drawing blood that they be without fear of bleeding her, for under her is a nerve. Therefore, if he adds to the incision for bleeding and finds a nerve, it happens that she becomes stunned, and her healing becomes difficult, and perhaps she does not heal at all.
And this nerve often appears to the sense of touch. But if in some people it is hidden and subtle and does not show itself, then you should place your bleeding incision where you will bleed her, with the section of Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If a vein is between two nerves, then find the vein along its length.
About the cephalic vein. The benefit of bleeding the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein, if you wish, to bleed her by pricking with an olive-wood lancet or a myrtle-wood lancet, because it is the safest of all veins, because there is no artery beneath it, nor a nerve.  />
Nevertheless, it is suitable for you when bleeding, that you avoid with the lancet only the head of the biceps, and seek a softer place: and it will not harm her if it is not found on the first strike, to repeat the bleeding over her several times.  />  />
Nevertheless, perhaps it swells in some people when not bleed on the first strike, but that swelling does not harm them in any way.
About the usefulness of bleeding and the precautions to be observed in it. The nature and effects of bleeding are from that which you should consider for its correction.
First, therefore, is what you should know: that bloodletting is not done except for the preservation of health, and its persistence, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And let bloodletting be done in one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Fusca, and the Basilic. And let bloodletting be done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach; in summer and autumn after eating, when there are signs of fullness.
And let bloodletting be done on the first or third day, after three hours have passed from midday.
However, bloodletting that is administered for illnesses does not have a fixed time, but whenever the circumstances and necessity demand it, whether at night or day, at any hour, and in any season.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should elderly people who have passed sixty years be bled.  />
Therefore, when someone wants to be bled for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels before the bloodletting with a gentle enema, if there is a lot of retained feces in them, so that the veins do not draw putrid harmful substances from the intestines during the bloodletting into the principal members.  />  />
Nor should a patient be bled who is suffering from nausea, or drunkenness, or recovering from drunkenness, until that condition is removed from them. Nor [should one bleed] a sick person with dropsy.
And one should avoid bleeding again after an attack of colic, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, labor, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, or anything else that diminishes strength from the body or animal nature.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that: if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines; if it is possible for him to do so.
Then let him enter the bath, unless something prevents him; or he should exercise in some way to refine the blood.
And place his phlebotomy in the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said; and strive that his chest be emptied that day of all evil accidents of the soul, such as anxiety, anger, and fear; and of all bodily accidents, such as labor, pain, superfluities, and similar things.
And he should have present those things whose use customarily runs in the lives of men for their fragrance, scent, and merriment, and similar things, according to what is possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a chair that is higher than the chair of the one being bled; then he should draw out his arm and rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tie the band with a cord and wrap it twice.
And let the tightness be moderate, for if the tightness is uneven or too tight, then it prevents the flow of blood; and if it is too loose, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after tying, it is necessary for the one being bled to rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the place of the bleeding with a small amount of old oil properly.
Then let him place his left index finger slightly above the vein in the place where he wants to make the incision, so that the vein does not flee and the strike is not missed. For there are veins that flee like a string during phlebotomy, and there are those filled with air; and when you place the incision on them, they contract under the incision, and deceive the phlebotomist, and the incision does not open the vein; and if it opens it, it is constricted. Therefore, it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and attentive in all these matters.
Then put aside the phlebotomy. If, therefore, the vein is opened with one stroke, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it with another stroke under that place, or above the same with haste; if the place does not suppurate.
If it suppurates, or if the patient is alarmed, then leave him for a day, or two days; and do not let the ligature be too tight. For perhaps a hot abscess will be drawn out. Let him not enter a bath. ã€‹ Neque comedat cibum nimis frigidum, nec calidum, nec siccum, nec umidum: sed mediocriter temperatum.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small or if the blood flows finely and you fear that not enough will come out, then repeat the phlebotomy in the same hole along its length, and add a little to the operation.
And do this with haste, before the place suppurates, for a suppurating place is common among many people during a small operation.
If you see that it has already suppurated, then do not repeat at all on top of it, for nothing will be excused to you. And put some olive residue on it, for it will help the flow of blood. ã€‹ Hoc faciet medicus in omni operatione sanguis. And this is better in this place than oil itself and any other kind of oil.
And likewise administer olive residue in every phlebotomy where you forbid the flow of blood; and they do this with theriac, with alfarach, and with saffron, when any one of them is placed on the spot.
For blood becomes thin and dissolves when it is thick.
But if a large abscess occurs in the place of phlebotomy - for this often happens, especially to those who are not bled except on that occasion or whose vein opening is small - then hasten and put a sponge soaked in salt water over the spot, heated slightly; and let it remain there for an hour, for it dissolves. ã€‹ Quod si non soluitur, tunc scinde eam et pone aliud super ipsum: quoniam et illud faciet idem.
And it is necessary that you do this after the flow of blood from that vein, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the place after several days, it does not harm; and if you wish, put something of dissolved aloe or myrrh on it, or some juice of mint, that is, calamint or something similar to it.
And indeed, many times pustules and swellings occur at the basilic vein phlebotomy.
Therefore, place your hand on it; if you find softness upon pressing it, then that swelling is bad. Be careful not to put anything of what we mentioned on it. For perhaps blood from the artery will flow out from there.
But make a plaster for it with what has an astringent property, so that the place may harden. Then care for him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract blood from every man according to his strength. And it appears from the prevailing color of the blood.
For if the blood is black, then allow it to flow until it turns red; and similarly if you see that it is thick, then let it flow until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary that he who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once a year should have an ample bloodletting of the vein, and the phlebotomy should be broad; and in him who is weak, it should be the opposite.
And it is necessary that he should be bled at intervals, and the opening should be narrow; and better than what is done in bloodletting by venesection is a transverse cut, not a prick.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from excessive bleeding and from cutting the nerve, and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting by width and length.
And if one is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, it is necessary that before the bloodletting they eat a moderate amount of bread soaked in pomegranate juice or wine if it is heated; and extract from them blood in three or four times.
And if they have a cold constitution, then before bloodletting let them eat bread soaked in apple syrup or in honey syrup flavored with spices, or in fragrant scented syrup.
But if fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is much blood loss, then it is necessary that they drink meat water and a subtle fragrant syrup; and let them use aromatics with algalia, and anoint their chest with it; and use the other things we have mentioned in the division of the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
But whoever wants to extend their arm and release their blood a second time, if the bloodletting is for much evacuation and his strength is weak, he should release the blood gradually according to the amount of his strength over continuous days.
But whoever wants to extend their arm and release their blood a second time, and their body is strong, then let them do it after seven or nine hours from their first bloodletting.
But whoever wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side to which it inclines, they should extend themselves on the second or third day.
In whose body the blood is already heated and sharpened, and causes fever to happen, it is necessary that blood come out of them in one go, and a large amount be drawn from them; and the opening be widened until fainting occurs, after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and place your hand on their pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not occur instead of fainting. Often it happens that the phlebotomist is unaware and falls into neglect.
And it is not appropriate when one wants to release the arm and let out blood a second time, after the vein's opening has already been closed, making the exit of blood difficult, to press strongly on it with force and quickly stretch out the arm. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it alone until it is bled again a second time.
Or remove the clotted blood from the vein's opening with a sharp phlebotomy instrument, and place some salt dissolved in water on it. And place on it some of the theriac called Alfaroc or Sagzena, and cover it with a gentle bandage until the blood comes out.
But if the vein has already formed an abscess, then leave it alone and do not cover it until the abscess is settled.
If the necessity compels one to let out blood a second time and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize at that same place or in another arm in another vein.
The cord of the arm is phlebotomized at the place of the Fusca and the Basilica, when they are not found and are hidden, because it is composed from them.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is such that the patient places his hand in warm water until it becomes hot like fire and the vein appears clearly.
Then tie a bandage around it with moderate tightness. Then phlebotomize transversely little by little, not broadly, nor longitudinally; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize it a little above the wrist joint.
If the exit of blood is prevented, then place your hand in warm water again and let the blood run in the water until you achieve what you need.
If however you are in the days of summer, then you should avoid washing your hands frequently in hot water. And indeed the course of blood is most placed in hot water in the time of winter.
And phlebotomy from this vein is safest of all veins because there is no pulsating vein beneath it nor a nerve.
But the phlebotomy of the right handâ€™s salutary vein is beneficial for illnesses of the liver.

Therefore, when you have drawn out the necessary amount of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not heal quickly.
And likewise, you should do this with every branch tied off.
The benefit of this phlebotomy from the left hand is that it is beneficial for illnesses of the spleen; and likewise do so with its phlebotomy as you did previously equally.
The benefit of saphenous phlebotomy is for illnesses in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the uterus, and retention of menses, and diseases of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the patient places his foot in warm water and makes a rubbing on it until the veins swell, and then binds above the ankle with a cord. And the place of the veins is at the heel, appearing towards the big toe, and many branches extend from it on the sole of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch or at the heel in its junction, for this is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it on the sole of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are beneath it and extend over the sole of the foot.

But the place of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is at the heel on the inner side.

Therefore, when it appears, then phlebotomize it in whatever way is possible for you, either by transverse incision or by longitudinal section, and the latter is better. The place of it is safer, and this thing is very hidden in many people.

Then release the constriction and place the skin over the site of the phlebotomy, and bind the site. For it heals quickly.
Â¶ On Cupping and the Benefit of Its Use.
Cupping glasses are made from horn, metal, or glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. ã€‹ One way is with scarification and bloodletting, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this type of cupping that is done without scarification is done in two ways. Either it is with fire, ã€‹ ã€‹ Or it is without fire.
And the sites for cupping that are given with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen places on the body. One is the cupping of the occiput, which is at the back of the head; and Alkehel, which is in the middle of the neck; and cupping of Alhagdahim, which are two sites on either side of the neck at the same time; and cupping under the jawbone below the mouth; and cupping of the two shoulders; and cupping above the tailbone; and cupping of the two elbows in the middle of both arms; and cupping of both legs; and cupping of both knees.
Cupping does not draw blood from the small, dispersed veins in the flesh, and therefore their potency is not diminished as it is with bloodletting. And it is not fitting to administer cupping either with fire or without fire for any illnesses that are caused by fullness until the entire body has been evacuated.
If necessity calls for cupping due to illness or habit, we administer it at the beginning of the month, at the end, and in the middle, and at whatever time it may be.
That is to say, because there are some people who, when their blood increases, require its extraction through cupping, find headache and vertigo in their head.
And among them are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the site of their cupping. And among them are those whose laughter is increased. And among them are those who taste blood in their mouth; or the tongue swells and they spit out blood. And among them are those whose sleep is increased. xspace And among them are those who see blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to these in their sleep.
Therefore, when we see any of these things and especially if it is on the third day of the month, we then order cupping to be done after two or three hours have passed from midday.
The benefit of cupping the neck is that it alleviates heaviness in the head and the discharge into the eyes. But it should be done only after the evacuation of the entire body.
And this cupping is used in place of cephalic venesection.
And it is not permitted for those of cold temperament or with a head cold to use it. It causes great harm to him.
And because of that it is not appropriate for the elderly to use it, nor for those whose heads are afflicted with cold diseases.
But he who becomes accustomed to it continually has forgetfulness generated in him.
And because of that it is necessary for you to instruct the person cupping in bloodletting to briefly lower his hand with the cup towards below, fearing lest forgetfulness be generated.
Cupping or Alkehel is the place of dark bloodletting and basilica bloodletting, and because of that it benefits asthma, constriction of breath, rupture of the lung, coughing, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the cup of Alkehel be briefly applied, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.  /> Et oportet, ut sit ei purgatus intestinum.
Cupping however of alhagdahim benefits pains occurring in the head, and eye diseases, and hemiplegia, and perforation, and pain in the tooth roots: and it is the place for basilica bloodletting.
And it is necessary to instruct the person cupping in bloodletting not to press hard with the cup after scratching, lest he cut an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Cupping however under the chin benefits ulcers of the mouth and corruption of the gums and similar diseases which are in the mouth; and it stands in place of bloodletting at Ali Ehriq which is on both lips.
Cupping however with spoons benefits al-khafeefah, which occurs from congestion and heat.
Cupping however of the inner parts of the two buttocks benefits the supplement to bloodletting of the three veins, basilica and dark and cephalic; because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh, and those small veins draw from other larger veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And you should instruct the bloodletter not to proceed too much with the incision, because the place is bare of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
And the cupping glass that is applied to the anus benefits hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
And you should instruct the bloodletter to use a large cupping glass and one made of metal, because the place requires strong suction; perhaps delicate ones break, and it should be incised with a large cut.
But cupping glasses on the legs diminish greatly, because they draw blood from the whole body; and they benefit old pains in the kidneys, womb, bladder; they provoke menstruation, and benefit boils and amenorrhea; and they serve as a substitute for two saphenous bleedings and alchorbe.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to happen in many people.
And the benefit of cupping glasses on the Alchorbe is close to the benefit of two leg cups.
The quality of the position of the cupping glasses is that you should place the cupping glass in the beginning empty and draw with moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may come to the place with equal arrival; and do not cease from repeating and continuing this until you see the place already reddening and swelling, and the redness of blood appears; then scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the disposition of bodies.
For someone who has tender flesh and few pores, you should scarify him only once, not more, so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the bloodletter to enlarge the incision and make it a little deep; and let him regulate the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is a growth in the blood, then it is necessary that you incise him twice. The first incision opens the way for the blood to be made subtle and watery, while the second incision is used for a precise examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If however the blood is very foul, then a third incision should be made so that it reaches its end.
And finally, when we want to extract blood gradually or briefly, one incision is sufficient for us. If we want to extract much blood, we should use multiple incisions.
And if we judge that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to incise with a deep incision, and the end of the incision should be equal to the depth of the skin.
Â¶ What is that which must be administered from the oils at the site of incision, and what should the person being bled avoid?
In the case of one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary that the sites of incision be anointed with open, soothing, softening oils: if this is in the summertime, such as alkali oil, or violet oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seeds.
And if this is in the wintertime, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or safflower oil, and others similar to these.
But if the excess is thick and cold, then let the oil be bitter orange oil, or oil of balm, or oil of aloe, or oil of dill, and others similar to these.
If the person to be bled has large pores and soft flesh, then it is necessary that they be kept from oil, and it is necessary that these people rinse the sites of incision with rose water, or cold water, or solarium water, or pumpkin water, or purslane water, and others similar to these. /> Â¶
He whose blood is very moist should cleanse his wounds with vinegar or water of myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
As for him whose discharges are thick, he should cleanse his wounds with old wine or water of sumac, or decoction of anise or chamomile, and similar things.
And it is necessary that cleaning should be avoided in the bath or after leaving the bath; rather, it should be administered one hour or two hours after leaving the bath.
And it is not permissible for someone to sleep after cleaning.
What should be used to fumigate or bloodlet before cleansing?
And after that you should consider first whether the patient who has been fumigated or bloodlet is choleric and if sharpness and inflammation prevail over his blood; then he should receive cooling things, such as pomegranate seeds, and also endive with vinegar, lettuce, sowthistle, and mallow, and similar things.
And his food should be simple, and the meat of mutton or fowl, and seasoned foods with sour condiments, and similar things.
And if his constitution is cold, he should take in drink syrup of honey or sweet wine, and althaea made from seeds; and let him take medium-aged fragrant wine, which is between old and new; and it is particularly recommended that he use a small amount of food. And let his food be simple and let him eat quail or turtledoves; and small birds like sparrows; and young pigeons.
And on the day of cleansing and bloodletting, drink should be more than food; and it is necessary to give in drink to certain people, in some hours, theriac alfaroch or a medicine made from musk, that is, mastic or aloeswood, before cleansing: and before or after bloodletting: so that the main members may be strengthened, and the blood refined. And it is not necessary for them to take it in a heated drink.
Of cupping without scarification. Now cupping without scarification are the cuppings placed on the liver, and spleen, and gout, and on the belly, and navel, and kidney region, and the bladder: because these members do not tolerate scarification on them.
And through them is sought nothing except drawing of blood from one member to another, as we place the cupping on the breasts during the illness of nosebleed. And we use them so that we may dissolve from the member the cold and unseen windiness in the member, as we place the cupping on the belly and navel. For it makes the member porous and warms it, and removes pain on account of this, because it dissolves that windiness.
Also it is placed on the kidneys when there occurs in them a stone or bladder stone. By the power of its drawing indeed perhaps it opens up the stone or roots out the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen for dissolving windiness in both.
And these cuppings are administered empty with only a cut. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water during pleurisy illnesses: and this is done so that the cupping be filled and large with only hot water or water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
The form of the cupping administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingers' breadth, as we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be on its side in the place which is about the middle of it a small hole, according to the size that can enter into it a needle: let it be made at once from brass or copper, thick-rimmed, smooth, even, polished so that it does not harm the member upon its position over it: and let there be in its midst a transverse rod of brass or iron where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this wound becomes large and swollen, larger than the one we have described, and only slightly smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of the person using it.
For indeed, the ventoses for boys and thin people are different from those for men and those with thicker bodies.
The manner of applying this ventose with fire on the limb is as follows: Light a wick made from linen or a small candle made from wax, and place it on the middle of the crosspiece that is in the center of the ventose, so that the fire burns around the lower part of the ventose, to prevent the patient's body from being burned.
Then place it on the limb, and let your finger be on the opening that I mentioned, until when we hold the ventose; while we need to, remove the finger, and the vapor will come out of that opening, and the ventose will immediately release.
Then light the wick as needed, and repeat the process if you require it again.
However, in the ventose used in the illness of pleuresis with water, there is no crosspiece nor opening; and it is only applied after being filled with water and placed on the limb.
And this is its form.
And the larger this ventose is, so that it can hold much water, the better it is.
Â¶ About the Suspension of Bloodletters.
Bloodletters are not used on many [parts of the body], except for those limbs where the application of ventoses is impossible due to their smallness, such as the lip and gum, and similar parts. Or on limbs that are bare of flesh, like the fingers, nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: that you come from bloodletters which are in sweet and clean waters, free from decay. â€º
Then they are released day and night into fresh water until they are hungry, and there is nothing left in their stomachs. Then the body should be first evacuated with phlebotomy and with enema. Then cleanse the member of the patient until it reddens. Then they are placed upon it.
When they are filled and fall off, and if it is possible to suck the place with an enema, then it is more useful; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, let it be washed and squeezed out.
If the flow of blood continues after the fall of leeches, and if this is due to perspiration, then pour a flax cloth into cold water and place it on top until the sweating stops.
If the blood increases, then sprinkle on it powdered alum or oak galls and similar astringents until the bleeding stops.
Or place half-peeled beans on the spot and let them stay until they stick to the spot, for the blood will stop.  \\
And if it is necessary to repeat the use of leeches, when possible, change them.
But if leeches are prevented from biting, then cleanse the spot with fresh blood. Or prick with a needle until some blood comes out. Then place them on, for they will stick immediately when they sense something from the blood.
When you want them to fall off, sprinkle some powdered aloe or salt or ash on them, and they will immediately fall off.
This chapter is also often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it concerns the restoration of bone fractures and dislocations.
Know, O Sons, that fools and the common people boast about this chapter, even though they have never looked into an ancient book nor read a single letter from it.
And for this reason, this method has been privately concealed among the sciences in our land. And I have never found anything good in it at all, and I would not have attained what I have attained except through the lengthy study of ancient books and diligence in understanding them, until I extracted that knowledge from them.
Then I devoted my life to experiments and long study.

Chapter 2
In the prologue of this chapter, it is fitting to summarize the key points and topics that are necessary for understanding them first: so that you, and those who are quick to learn, may know or learn this noble art, and teach others from you.
I say, therefore, that when someone has a fracture, dislocation, twist, or fall, you should hasten first to bleed them and loosen them, or both together, unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength: or the one who experienced something of these things is a child, or an old man withered by age: or it is a time of very hot weather, or very cold. ï¸Ž
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and birds or goat meat; and avoid wine, heavy meats, and overeating of all foods that fill the veins with blood. Until you are sure of the healing of the warm swelling, and you do not fear the outpouring of matter to the member; then let the sick man return to his first regime with which he was accustomed.
When the fracture of the mouth begins to heal, then it is necessary for the sick person to be fed with nourishing foods that are abundant in nutrients and thick; such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, rice, fish heads, fish tails, cow intestines, eggs, fresh fish, and heavy wine, and similar things. With this diet, the healing of the fracture is faster and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the first stage of old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be joined and healed according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their members; and they heal and join together those which are in the end of softness, just like the bones of little children.
However, nature attaches to the broken bone from every side something similar to glue, which has thickness, by which it adheres and binds together until they are joined together and bound together until it reaches the end of strength and firmness as it was at first, so that nothing prevents it from performing its functions; and because of this cause, it is necessary to provide for the sick person foods in which there is heaviness, thickness, and abundance, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members; for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all other members are diverse from one another. And I have come with the recall of every type of fracture described in its own chapter, distinguished from others. And the types of bone fractures are also diverse among themselves.
Since a fracture can be curved, besides which splinters may occur in it. And sometimes the fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and these fractures are fragmented, that is, splinters, and protruding pieces, like those in a reed when it is trimmed with a knife for writing. And they do not protrude: and there is a fracture accompanied by a wound and tearing at the end: and there is a small fissure fracture.
And every type has its own skill or tool for its repair, as will be mentioned later, arranged by topic.
And from that which is recognized as a bone fracture is its twist and prominence, and its appearance to the touch, and its sound when you press it with your hand.  />
When there is no apparent twist or sound to the touch when you move the bone, and the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not a fracture. Rather, it may be a twist or a small fissure. Therefore, do not disturb it with your hand or pressure. Instead, place on it some of the medicines whose use will be mentioned after, when the time is right for that: then bind it tightly with fine bandages.
And know that when a place is curved and split into two, besides which splinters occur in it, however, every part is removed from its pair: then you should begin to straighten it immediately, evening it out before an inflammation occurs.
But if an inflammation occurs in it, then leave it for some days until the inflammation subsides.  />  /> Then straighten it according to whatever method is possible with gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and equalization are easier than that of bone, where fractures occur.
Then tie it according to how you remember it.
But if there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two sides, whether it be hand or foot; either with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an equal spot, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended; then strive with all your skill and in whatever manner you can to return those additions to their places: and beware lest by your operation any pain or injury occur to the weak spot. And strive with all your skill that you join one of the two bones with its mate, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time that you feel and touch it with your hand. Therefore, if you see something different there, correct it and make it equal according to the extent of your skill.
And beware of making a violent extension or a strong compression, as many foolish ones do.  /> For often they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation; as we have already seen this from their operations many times.
Then bind it after equalization and proper operation, stricture, rest, and tranquility; and admonish the patient about this. "When he feels pain or injury in the position of a limb, then it is necessary to change its position to one that causes no pain.
"And be careful with him so that his position is equal and straight, so that the limb does not become twisted when it is restored.
"And the condition of a broken limb's constriction is as I describe.
"Know that broken limbs are varied by their smallness, and their size, and their shape.
"Therefore, those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and thigh, then it is necessary for there to be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. "But those that are thick, such as the thigh and leg, there should be a broad, hard wrapping.
"Because the wide bandage binds the large limb and constrains it from all sides equally with even tightness, so that no blemish enters it.
"Then begin after your evacuation from the equal position to place on top of the broken area an appropriate ointment on soft tow. "Then begin to wrap the bandage over the broken area with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves, and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
"Then go with it to the upper part from the broken place, and tighten less than you tightened the broken place. "Then extend yourself with the wrapping from the broken place slightly, and gradually ease the tightness until you reach a large healthy part.
"Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken place with wraps. "After that, go with the wrapping to the lower part from the break. "And let your work be in the tightening of the wrapping and its easing, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the layers of soft tow or cloth, with which the twisting of the fracture is made even, if there is any twisting in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another layer over it. Afterwards, place on these layers evenly made and well-shaped splints immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess in the place.
But if there is swelling or abscess in it, then place on it something that will relieve the abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then tie the splints over it.
And let the splints be made from half-round canes, planed and wisely shaped; or they may be made of wood used for sieves, which are made from pine or geraid, that is, branches of the palm tree, or calingi: and let the width of each splint be according to this form and figure.
However, it is necessary that the splint placed on the tracture be somewhat thicker and wider than the other splints; and the length of the splint should be according to what is allowed by the member in terms of size and narrowness.
Then tie another bandage over the splints, similarly to the first binding. Then tie it securely with threads, as we have said about the binding; and know that your binding over the site of the fracture should be more, and the farther it extends from the fracture, the less the binding.
And know that the threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish people who use their threads made from twisted flax cords, it is a great mistake; for the binding slips with them outside of the proper quality.
And fine threads are not suitable again because you will not achieve through them what you want due to narrowing.
And it should not be less than a finger's width between the stitches.
If the patient is injured by the tips of the stitches after constriction in healthy places, then place under them some soft tow or carded wool until he is no longer harmed by it.
When there is a fracture with a wound and disruption of the skin occurs, I will excuse myself from mentioning it again by its singularity in the second chapter.
And know that not every broken bone should be tied with stitches on the first day. Which means that if the bone is large, you should not place stitches on it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the occurrence of a hot swelling.
And this is the method of plasters by which the ancients used to restore, and which are placed on a fracture or dislocation.
The method of a common shortened plaster for restoring a fracture, suitable for most constitutions, especially women and children, because it is made from something that neither heat nor cold can overcome.

Another method for making a plaster that is also beneficial for fractures and dislocations: Take equal parts of mugwort, marshmallow, and white hellebore, each one ounce; also take myrrh and both kinds of aloe, each half an ounce; and Armenian bole, two ounces. By this procedure nourishment comes to the member, and it is healed quickly.

On the making of an injury to the head.
There are various kinds of fractures and many shapes; their causes are also distinct.
For there is a fracture caused by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely to where it reaches the marrow, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and on account of this, this type of fracture is called 'axial'.
Or the sword cuts into part of a bone and cuts its surface but does not penetrate all the way through; and this type of fracture is called 'kalaan', that is, 'complete incision'.
And there are two types of wounds of fractures: large or small. Â¶
And among the fractures is 'crushing' or 'grinding', and its cause is a blow with a stone or falling onto a stone or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating and approaching the marrow which is under the bone, or it is on the surface of a bone; and there are two types of wounds of these fractures: small or large.
And among the fractures is one which is hidden within a bone, like a hair's breadth, and it is a small crack; and on account of this it is called 'capillary'. Â¶ â€Œ
And among them is the fracture which occurs from a fall or blow with a stone and similar things, which cause a layer of bone to split towards the inside, creating a depression, as happens in bronze cauldrons when they are struck, and part of them splits towards the inside.
And this occurs most frequently in the heads of moist bones, such as those of children.
And for all these types of fractures, splints are separated, and I will come with a reminder of the whole in its place.
And we know all these types of fractures by examination upon them, and investigation with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
But the species of the capillary is recognized by this, that it is exposed above the skin, and cleaned, and covered with wax: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms: then if you see from the symptoms something that indicates a clear danger, such as vomiting of bile, distention, loss of intellect, hoarseness, fainting, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, their redness, and similar symptoms in various conditions, it is certainly not cured: and if you see non-threatening symptoms and hope for recovery, then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded person comes to you at the time of wounding: and it happens in the winter days, then you must strive for the removal of bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the summer days, then you must hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane corrupts, and those symptoms we have mentioned occur.
But if the fracture of bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with fragments, and drawing inward: then you must cut away the broken and drawn part, as I tell you.
And it is that you shave the head of the wounded person: and expose the bone in whatever way possible for you: according to the shape of the wound, and what is easiest on the patient.
If during the extraction of a bone there is bleeding or a hot abscess, then apply that which is necessary. And it is that you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and make the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be secure from bleeding. Then begin with the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a trephine of sharp edge, and this is its form.
Then administer another trephine after the first, wider than it by a little; and this is its form.
Then administer also another trephine, even wider than the second one.
And the second method is that you should have with you a number of different trephines, some of which are wider than others and some shorter; and they should be sharp at the end of their extremities, and made of Indian iron or steel; and use facility in striking over the trephine, so that it does not shake the head and injure it.  />
But if the bone happens to be hard and strong, then it is necessary that you should bore around it before you administer the trephines with boring instruments called non-penetrating borers; and they are not so named except because they themselves do not penetrate the end of the bony substance to what is behind it; for this reason, since the end of the borer is rounded over that which is under its sharp point, like a border and small circle, it prevents sinking in and penetrating the thickness of the bone.  /> />
And it is fitting for you to take from these borers a great many, each of which fits the thickness of the bone, until you have with you a borer for every skull; and the length and shortness of the sharp end of its tip should be according to the thickness of that particular skull.
And this is the form of three types of Terebrae, or Trepanes.
The quality of penetrating around the broken bone is this: you place the Terebra on the bone and roll it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the Terebra to another place and put between every hole a quantity of thickness of the radius or about that amount.
Then cut with the Incisors what is between every hole from the bone and make that thinner than you can over that to the point of fineness, as we said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other of those instruments which you have prepared for it, such as tweezers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you take care with all caution that the Terebra or Incisors touch nothing of the brain.
Therefore when you go around the bone by penetrating and have separated it from the brain if it was continuous with it, and after you have extracted it, it is necessary that you scrape and even out the end of what remains in the bone with another similar instrument like the Incisors. However, it ought to be thin and fine more than other Incisors.
If something remains from the small bones and splinters then take those easily with that which you have among your instruments. Then cure the wound afterwards with compresses and ointments as we said.
And another method of operating is very easy and far from fear, which Galen said and praised it greatly, and this is his statement. Then strike over the Incisor with a small hammer from one side until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it is turned: and you will be confident from the cloth that no accident will happen to it which you fear completely.

But in each of the remaining small cracks and subtle fractures, it is necessary to take care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a thing that is not hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and we have written about it and demonstrated in cases of great fractures.
But if the skin flap is retained when you extract the bone: then it is necessary to take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth, either double or triple-folded, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth. Therefore, if such afflictions befall the patient, know that it is undoubtedly fatal.

Â¶ On the Restoration of the Nose When It Is Broken.
Know that only the two upper parts of the nose are broken simultaneously or one of them, because they are two bones; for the lower part is cartilage and cannot be broken, and nothing happens to it except for abrasion, twisting, and flattening.
If one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril and equalize that fracture with your index finger and thumb from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; and this should be done skillfully and easily.  /> And take care that no pain occurs to the patient due to your action.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it; then it should be equalized with the tip of a small stick.
But if the fracture is in both parts, then do likewise; and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day during the quiet stage of a hot swelling.
Then insert a linen wick into the tip of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two wicks if the fracture is in both parts; and let the thickness of the wick be such that it fills the nostril.  /> Â•
And some of the ancient surgeons said that ointments should be poured into butter and changed every day. However, this does not seem right to me; rather, the ointment should be poured into a bladder of an egg and mixed with ground pepper. Then leave the ointment until the bone hardens and the cartilage thickens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of ointment, after wrapping the nose in a soft cloth: and this happens so that it holds the broken nose more firmly, and so that the sick person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you want, do that; and if you want, make ointments.
But if a hot abscess occurs on the nose while you are working on it, make a plaster for the nose with caerot or cotyledon boiled in rose oil or a little of diacodion.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you should make plasters for it with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with an egg bladder. Then place soft wool on top of it, and do not bind the nose at all.
But if the broken bones of a small nose fracture are shattered, then you should cut through them and remove them with appropriate instruments. Then, whether it is a cut or a wound, take care of it with what causes the flesh to grow and strengthens from suitable plasters or ointments for that.
But if a wound occurs between the nostrils, then you should take care of it with ointments and administer a lead tube until it heals. âž¤
Â§ On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not with a wound, then see if it is only broken on the outside and is not split into two parts and curves toward the inside; then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert with your left hand the index finger into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left mandible, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and with it push the prominence of the fracture inward with ease from the inside with your other hand from the interior part of the bone neatly, and make it equal.
But if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should administer traction from both parts, according to the straight line, until it is possible to make them equal.
But if the shock or disruption has already occurred to the teeth, then bind them with gold wire or silver wire, or silk thread from which you hope they will remain. Then place a splint on the broken jaw. Then place on it two layers of cloth, and on the cloth a large neatly made bandage; or an equal piece of sole skin in the length of the jaw.
Then bind it from above according to how its bandage is prepared and its application is suitable until it does not break; and advise the patient to rest and be quiet; and give him soft food.
But if you think that something has changed in the shape in any way, then hurry and remove it on the third day. Then correct what has changed from it, and apply a plaster of mill dust with egg white or sesame flour, after you have removed the splint; and place soft wool on top of the plaster.
While that bandage remains on it and the bone's position does not change, leave it alone, lest you loosen it, until it heals; or bind up the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
But if a hot abscess occurs meanwhile, then administer what we have often said for its reduction, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider [this]. For if already a fragment is separated from the bone, either by itself or through carelessness; then be skillful in this, so that you remove those fragments of splinters along with what suits you among the instruments for their removal.
But if the wound's opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a fleam according to your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters and nothing remains of them, whether the wound's opening is then wide or notâ€”if it is not, place on it one of the bandages that suit it, and allow it to heal until it heals.
Â¶ On restoring the collarbone when it breaks.
Indeed, the collarbone most often breaks from its anterior part towards the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it either breaks and separates into two parts, apart from the fact that splinters may occur in it; and this is easier to restore.
Or [it happens] such that splinters occur in the bone, and this is more difficult to restore.  /> Or [it breaks] with a fragment of bone remaining attached to it; and this is most difficult to restore.
Or so that [the break] occurs with a wound.
And the procedure in it when the break is without a wound is to have two assistants present: so that one of them holds support for the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards the affected side.  /> Then you place the fingers of your right hand under the broken end [of the collarbone], while with your left hand you push the healthy end upwards.  /> And you continue doing this until the broken ends join together perfectly.  /> Then you bind them tightly with a bandage, and leave it for fifteen days or so, not allowing anyone to touch it.
Then make the fracture even with your fingers until it takes on its proper form: and there should be no projection or depression.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place under the patient's scrotum a pad of cloth or wool: and its size should be according to your needs: and extend and raise the Furcula, and compress the pad with your hand, until you make the fracture even, as required.
If you cannot pull the end of the Furcula outward because it has gone too deep, then it is necessary that the patient be turned over onto his back: and place under his shoulder a medium-sized pad, and have your assistant press down on the shoulder until the bone of the Furcula which is deep comes up: and then correct the fracture and make it even with your fingers.
But if you feel a splinter from the Furcula that moves, then it is necessary to scrape over it and remove the splinter easily.
If however it is retained in the bone, then use an awl in its incision with one of the instruments that you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed under the Furcula an instrument that preserves Sifach: and this instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a spoon without a bowl.
And its width should be according to your need for it, in proportion to the size and thickness of the bone. Its length also according to what is possible for you in working with it.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
If however the gap of the wound that you cut when you removed the bone splinter is wide and you are safe from inflammation, then bring together the two lips of the wound with a stitch.
And if the wound is small or you suspect a warm abscess, then fill the wound with cloths and fine wool according to the size of the wound.
If a warm abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on the wound.
But a stricture of the bone without a wound or incision should be treated by placing on it an ointment made from flour and egg white. And place soft tow on top of it. Then place a weight under its heel if necessary.  /> Then take a bandage, that is, a roll as the Saracens use, which should be very long; its width should be one palm or about that. Then place a doubled piece of wool on top of the tow and ointment. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width should be three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the splint in the place of the bandage that falls over the break and the place. Then place the bandage over the break as it was rolled, and wrap it around its neck and under its sound heel and under its damaged heel; and bring it back over every part, so that the stricture holds the break well: and this should not be hidden from you, nor removed, lest the splint slip off the broken bone.  /> And therefore it is necessary that you examine the patient every day. Whenever the bandage softens and you see that the splint has already slipped off, then straighten it and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's back on his back, and place under his heel, by his bedside, a small pillow at night, so that with it his support is raised from his side. The fracture of the clavicle is healed by raising the arm.
Or tie the arm of that person to their own neck; and do not untie the bandage if it is not happening in the place of the injury due to itching or from a swelling, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster if you see the face turning towards it. And add tightness, and leave it until it is restored and solidified.
And indeed the fracture of the clavicle is strengthened and reinforced most in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens more quickly in certain individuals.
Â¶ On the healing of a scapula fracture.
The scapula is rarely broken, and only in its broad part; and from it nothing breaks except its edges.
When it is broken in that place, and it breaks in the middle of it, and this is not known except by touch; then according to how the fracture appears prepare it.  /> And bring it back to its natural form in every way possible for you.
Then place on the site powder of flour with the white of an egg and soft flax, and put a little bit of doubled cloth over it. Then place on it a broad splint made of thin board according to the size of the entire scapula, or slightly larger than that.
If however there is a hollow beneath the splint in any place of the scapula, then fill that hollow with soft flax until the splint descends to an even level.  />  />  â€¢
Then fasten over it with a long bandage, of decent tightness and firmness of the splint, so that it is not moved from its place.  />  â€¢
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage becomes loose, tighten it and if the splint is moved from its place, even out the splint; and let the patient lie on their sound side.
And a spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
Therefore, when this number is completed, then release the bandage, and you will be secure. For it [the bone] is of bones whose separation is not to be feared, nor their loosening.
But if it proceeds from a fragment of bone and pricks under the skin, then cut along with it and remove it. And do what we said about a fracture of the collarbone due to the subsidence of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Â¶ About the restoration of a rib fracture.
The chest is sometimes broken in its middle part, and this happens rarely. Its extremities are frequently broken and crushed by its accidents.
When its middle part is broken and bends towards the lower [part], and there is a severe pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps it vomits blood, and the concavity of the broken bone is noticeable to the senses, the restoration is that the patient should be made to lie on his back: and place a pillow between its blades. ã€‹
Then press its shoulders and gather its ribs from both sides or the flanks with hands; and make them equal in thickness according to all possible ways that are appropriate until the shape of the bone returns as it should be. ã€‹
Then place on it a plaster and wool, and place on top of them a splint made from thin wood such as willow or hazel, or similar light woods, after you have wrapped the [ribs] in cloth. Then make it even and tie it over the broken bone so that it does not move; and go with the bandage following its roundness to the back several times, and bind it tightly with a decent tightness. ã€‹
Then consider the ligature at any time, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if its necessity calls for its loosening due to itching or corrosion that occurs in the place, or pain, or an abscess: then hurry, and loosen it, and remove the poultice, and correct what happens with its method of treatment.
Then return the poultice if you see a pus-like substance; and attach to it a ligature until it heals.
About the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that the breaking of ribs does not occur except in thick places which follow the back, and from their ends on the front part. And they do not break except because they are made of cartilage. And the recognition of it is not hidden from the sense by examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done thus: that you make the broken part even with your fingers as much as possible, until it matches the shape, as is necessary. Then apply a poultice and bind the broken bone with a splint if it needs it. ã€‹
And if the break of the ribs is inclined towards the inside: then a severe pain and stabbing occurs, just like that which happens to one who has pleurisy; because the bone pricks the membrane. And also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood occurs; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have already devised many ingenious methods for it; and among them are those who say that you should give to the sick person foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the stomach is inflamed and swollen and the break is pushed outwards. ã€‹
But we absolutely reject doing this, lest a hot abscess be formed quickly if it is not already present. If however it has already happened, then he adds it in that (case).
And some of them said: let there be placed over the wound a bellows, then let it be sucked out with strength, and this is more similar to reasoning. Nevertheless, one should fear that the bellows will attract superfluities to the place, due to the weakness of the body's condition.
And some of them said: the wound should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil; and between the ribs there should be placed small pieces (of cloth) until it is filled, so that when it is wrapped around it will make an equal bandage according to its roundness.
Then the patient should be treated with a pleural cure made from food and medicine.
But if something violent happens to the patient, which he cannot bear, and his face is pricked with a piercing pain, and we fear for the patient's life; then it is necessary to cut open the place and expose the broken rib. Then we shall place over it the instrument that preserves the siphon, of which the description has been given previously; and we shall cut the bone with ease, and cut through it.
Then we shall join together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a stitch; and we shall treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then hasten and pour small pieces into rose oil, and place them over the site; and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for an abscess.
Meanwhile, he should also lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he recovers.
Â¶ On the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
For the bones of the neck, a fracture sometimes occurs, and rarely an abrasion; similarly also for the vertebrae of the back. When this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not heal, then observe. For if you see that the personâ€™s hands are already softened, numbed, and lifeless, and they cannot move them or extend them or clench them; and when you try to clench their fingers with your own and prick them with a needle, they do not feel it and experience no pain: then know that the condition is incurable and is often fatal.
But if they can move their hands and feel the clenching with your fingers and the pricking with a needle: then know that the neck of the bone is intact, and that the patient will heal with proper care.
But if the same thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will also heal or not: then observe the personâ€™s feet. For if they are softened and experience what was described for the hands; and when lying on their back, there is a release of wind and waste without control; and when turned onto their stomach, there is a release of waste without control; and when lying on their back and wish to urinate but cannot: then know that it is fatal.  /> Do not bother with treating him.
But if none of these things happen to him, then the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what happened is to try to reduce the hot swelling in this way: place crushed rose oil alone on the vertebra, or with fried egg yolks.
Apply this to the area several times a day until the hot swelling subsides; then place one of the strengthening and drying plasters on it and bind it firmly with a bandage. Advise the patient to rest and remain calm. He should not sleep on the part where he feels pain until it is healed.
If it happens at the site of a fractured bone or any detached part: then it is necessary to make an incision over the skin and remove that. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with stitches, if it is large. Then care for him with some ointment until he is healed.
But if the last bone of the tail is broken: then it is necessary to introduce your left thumb into the anus and align the broken bone with your other hand as best you can, and re-align after the first alignment.
Then place him on his side and remove it; and care for the wound with the preceding treatment until he is healed.
Â¶ On the restoration of the anus.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the anus are rarely broken.  /> If they are broken, then the break is not complete but a splintering at their extremities; and they split lengthwise and turn towards the inside; and pain occurs in that place with tenderness, and the leg that is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration is to place your hand over it until you are standing above the break as its shape shows.
If the break is only at its extremities, then align it as you prepare from the alignment until it conforms to its natural shape.  /> Â•
But if the break is lengthwise or has already turned towards the inside, then have the patient lie on his stomach until you are ready to restore that break. So when you have aligned it, place a plaster over it; then place over it a splint of wood or leather and bind it with a bandage that will not cause displacement of the break nor movement of the splint.
And make the cavity of those equal with that which fills it, until the stricture takes it equally; and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs: then cease from extending it and its restoration until the hot abscess settles; and place upon it what settles it, as was said before, then return to restoring it; and tighten it as necessary.
But if it occurs in a bony protuberance or is fractured, extract it.
Â¶ On the restoration of the fracture of the thumb.
The thumb is that which is between the elbow and the end of the palm.
Therefore, if it breaks, its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat wood of medium thickness, according to this shape which follows.
And two bands are bound at the two ends.
Then it is suspended from a high place; and the patient sits on a seat.  ?> Then he lets his broken arm fall onto the wood until it reaches its tip, where in the middle of which is attached a curvature of the wood.
