Then place something heavy on top of it so that the assistant extends it downwards. Then the physician sets the fracture with his hands at the same time until the fracture returns as necessary.
And the other method is that the patient lies on their back; and hang his hand from his own neck with a band. Then instruct two assistants to hold one of them what is above the fracture with his hands; and that the other holds below it, and each extends towards his own side.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with one band and above it with another; and each of the two assistants extends towards his own side.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligament be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a ligament be placed over that spot and over the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease and without effort until when the fracture is aligned as it should be and a good application is made: then tighten it, unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the bandage until the seventh day. And place on it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess quiets down: then tighten it: and the way to tighten it is to place a poultice over the fracture.
Then wrap a new cloth bandage over the poultice. Then raise the arm to the support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And wrap and bind the arm to the support so that the arm stands in place of a crutch: unless something prevents you or anything changes due to the broken bone.
But if you fear that something may change due to the broken bone, then use crutches: and it is that you should place a wider and stronger crutch over the fracture itself. And between each crutch, place a finger's width of space: and the length of the crutches should be according to the fracture with an additional three fingers from every side.  />
Then bind over the crutches the bandage I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.  />  />  />
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture extends, the less should the bandage be.
If you see the position of the stars and the constriction, as we have said, immediately in the hour when you restore the limb, then do this: but if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the constriction and the stars until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bond for three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, or food is prevented from reaching the limb because of the disposition of the superfluity of constriction. Therefore correct all of that, as we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bond until after three days, or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his hand on his belly; and place under his aid a cushion filled equally with wool.
And be mindful at every hour of the night or day, that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or the bond softened. Therefore correct all of this with your strength.
And give the patient's food according to the order that we have described previously, so that it is fine at first, until the mouth begins to dry out; then it is necessary to thicken his food.
For the custom of aid and leg is that it should be tight for forty days; then therefore it is necessary to loosen it, and administer a bath, and treat with ointments that correct this.
But if the break is ugly and ground down, then do not loosen from it the bond and stars until fifty days, and even two months.
Â¶ On the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearms.
Of these, one is smaller; and it is that which follows the thumb. â€Œ And another is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forceps breaks, or perhaps the smaller one; and perhaps they both break at once.
Therefore when the small upper forceps breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forceps breaks, its breakage is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse still when two bones are broken at once.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the small upper forceps, then it is fitting for the physician in its restoration to place a small extension with ease until he makes it even.
If however it is the large forceps that has been broken, then it is necessary to place a very strong extension.
And it is necessary for him to place the figure of the hand in its restoration, and its extension extended over the cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint of the thumb be higher than all the digits or others; and let the patient sit squarely on himself, and let the cushion be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height so that the patient does not incur labor.  />
Then let the assistant stretch the arm from below with his hand or with a bandage. And let another assistant extend it similarly from above.
Then let the physician make the bone even until he brings it back to the best shape he can.
If there are splinters in the bone break, then strive to bring each fragment back to its place with your strength.  />  />  />
But if it appears to be a separate splinter that pricks the skin and there is no hope for its restoration, then shave over it and remove it according to the manner we have said before.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter for that, therefore take the treatment from there.
If a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your healing, then take a cloth made from rose oil and white wax, with a consistency between thick and thin; bind the cloth over it with a fine bandage until the abscess subsides; then remove the wax, and apply an emplastrum prepared from millstone powder and egg white. Then place a compress, and let the compress that is placed directly over the break be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of compresses for the arm is six, arranged in various ways; whether the break is in one or two joints at once.
Then place your bandage over the break stronger and more vigorous, and somewhat tighter towards the upper part of the break or its lower part; or place a softer bandage for a short time, as has been mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped around the break be soft and moist; and not hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be made of linen, medium between fine and thick, as we have said.
And examine the member and its ligaments after days. If anything happens that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the member; then it is necessary to bathe the member with warm water until that itching subsides, and leave the member unbound until it subsides; then renew the bandage.
If the bandage has already been softened, and the bone has been separated, and similar things; then strengthen that whole with your skill, and examine again.
For if food is prevented from reaching the member because of the excess tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it somewhat and leave it for some days until the food can reach it; then bind it again.
If however nothing happens to the patient from those things which we have mentioned, then it is not necessary that he be bled, except after twenty days or thereabouts.
Then let the hand of the patient be suspended around his neck. And let his neck be straight and guarded by its strength from violent movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days, or in thirty-two days, and perhaps it is healed in eighteen days. All of that depends on the temperaments of the body and the strength of the patient.
Â¶ On the Healing of Fractures of the Hand.
Know that a fracture of the bones of the hand and of the joints of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not occur unless there is frequent friction.
Therefore, when a fracture or attrition of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the patient sit with his back straight, and before him let there be a seat according to its measurement. Then place his hand upon it extended.  /> Si autem accidit infirmo fractura digitorum, tunc oportet, ut ponatur infirmus supinus: et quiescent digiti sui in terra.
Then let the assistant stretch the broken bones, and let the physician straighten them until they are joined together by a good union.
Then it is necessary to place a plaster and wool if there does not occur a hot swelling. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place, and you have now rolled it up in a linen cloth.
But if the fracture is near the bottom of the sole of the foot, then make something similar to a ball from cloth, and instruct the patient to press on it with his broken sole; then let it be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, which has softness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the sole; and bind it as is necessary.
If, however, the fracture is on the outer side, then you should place a splint on top and another splint from below on the sole of the foot; and let the hand be held open and straight. Then put a bandage around it, as the hand is wrapped: and stretch it between the fingers with a ligament.
If a fracture happens to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be set as is proper. Then let it be bound with a splint: and if you wish, put a small straight splint on it so that the fracture may be lifted up and not moved.
But if the fracture is in any of the other fingers, such as the middle finger, and index finger, or ring finger, and little finger, then let it be set and bound with the healthy finger that follows it; or let them all be bound together according to their continuation: and this is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And take into account in your restoration and after the arrangement of the warm compress: and apply what is needed when something from it happens, according to its narrative.
Â¶ On the restoration of a hip fracture.
The hip bone is often broken and becomes apparent to the senses because it turns inward and backward.
And its restoration is done thus: that a bandage is tied above the fracture, and another bandage below the fracture. â€º And let the patient be laid on his back. â€º â€º
Then let every bandage be extended by the assistant to its respective place according to equality; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone. â€º â€º â€º â€º
But if the fracture is near the base of the hip, then let the bandage be at the pubic bone so that the extension goes upward; and another bandage below the fracture.
And thus likewise if there is a fracture near the knee, then let there be a ligament near the knee, so that it may extend to the lower parts.
Then the physician should adjust the bone with both his hands until he restores it to its natural form: and join the bone with a good union.
Then you must place a poultice and bandage, if an abscess has not occurred in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the hot abscess settles; then return to your treatment.
And its bandage should be like this: You must wrap over the fracture a hard bandage, that is, a splint; and double it twice or three times, and let there be an excess of it. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the base of the calf muscle.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee towards the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper part on both sides. Then wrap over the leg and thigh what is in excess of the bandage.
Then place on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself a splint, and put one splint over the shinbone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bandage may be even.
Then begin strongly with the bandage from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wraps. And as you extend more with the bandage from the site of the fracture, let your bandage be smaller and softer and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg. ã€‹ Tie therefore with both three splints each which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread towards the lower part until you reach the heel of the foot. And tie both ends of the strings to the other side: so that the ligature is not removed from its place.
Then leave the bandage on it until it heals, so that no decay, abscess, or swelling, and similar things occur in the limb. If anything happens from this. Therefore hasten with its removal, and correct whatever happened from it entirely, as we have said many times.
If however there is a splinter of bone that is piercing, then it is necessary to equalize it, if it is possible for you. And if not, place a bandage over it and remove it; and care for it as we mentioned before, until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is tied with strings, just as we said for the arm and hand.
However, in the healing of such a fractured one, lameness does not happen to the invalid. And if it is restored alone, without the other leg being applied to it, then it is necessary for that one to always limp.
And know that the hip is tied for fifty days, or a little more, or a little less; all of this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other dispositions.
Â¶ On the healing of the fracture of the patella of the knee.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap happens rarely, but its abrasion occurs often.
If however it does happen to be fractured, then only either a fissure or a hole in its parts.  /> Et si est fracta Rotula, tunc debet medici cognoscere, utrum est fracta tota, aut fracta pars eius. And it is either with a wound or without a wound. And stand on it entirely with the sense of touch.
And its restoration is thus: that which was disrupted from its parts with fingers, until it is aggregated and joined together as much as possible by equality and simplicity, and construction.
Then place a plaster made of sesame flour or from a mill with egg white; and place on top of it a round piece of cloth if you need it; and bind over it an appropriate bandage for that.
Then consider all the arrangements we have mentioned for other fractures; such as a hot abscess and similar things; so that you oppose every incident with what is suitable for it, until it heals.
Â¶ About the restoration of a thigh fracture.
Know that the thigh has two bones, one of which is large and is called by the name 'thigh bone', and the other is fine and is called 'tibia'.
And it happens to both types of fracture that it happens to the two bones of the arm; and because of this its restoration is done like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones break at the same time, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the finer bone breaks, the leg turns to the front.
And if the larger bone breaks, the leg becomes shorter; and it is one of those which do not conceal itself from you.
Then administer extension, equality, and bandaging of the cloths according to that equally.
However, it is necessary, if the thigh fracture is a foul fracture, having many splinters; that the extension be less and gentler with your alleviation.
And in the case of the thigh there is something more to do than for the arm; and that is, when you have made the cloths equal and completed all your work, then take two strips of wood from pine which are used in the construction of sunrooms placed under the boards: and they are made into palm-sized pieces; and choose those whose thickness is not too great nor are they among the smallest; and their length should be according to the length of the thigh from the knee downwards.  />
Then wrap around each cloth twice along its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the other side; and they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind two bandages in three places: at both ends and in the middle. For when constriction is prevented, the leg deviates to the right and left, and it stands firmly in place.
And now indeed let a cradle be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and place the leg in it so that it may preserve it from motion.
And indeed you must do this especially in a fracture when there is an associated wound.
Then examine the leg every day, and strive with all your might to prevent swelling or inflammation, or any other complications.  () If therefore any of these things happen, then treat it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills it.  ()
On the Fracture of the Bones of the Foot and Toes.
Indeed, the heel bone never breaks completely.
However, the bones of the foot sometimes break, and the toes hardly ever break; and this does not happen to them except by abrasion in many cases.  () ():
If the bones of the foot break and you see those bones separate from one another, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground as if he were walking.  () ():
Then stand on your own foot and place it over the one that is elevated from those bones; afterward press or step on it, and adjust it until it returns to its proper place.
Then apply a poultice and wool on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat ends.
Then bind it with a proper ligature after you have wrapped it with bandages and other necessities.
After three or four days have passed, then loosen the ligature. For you will find that the bones are leveled, whether they were broken or dislocated. The operation is therefore on them, as you see.
But if some of the fingers are broken, then restore them, and level them as I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the finger according to its length, and let it be slightly wider; then place this board that I have told you under the ligature, and bind it with a proper ligature.
But if two, or three, or more of the bones are broken, then place a splint on each finger according to its size, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board below the ligature: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the ligature, so that it holds well.
And it is fitting for you not to forget to consider all that I have told you in the other fractures from the accidents that we mentioned; and oppose every accident with what is necessary.
Â¶ On the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the pubic bone, and a man's penis.
When a womanâ€™s vulva is broken, then make her sit on a stool. Then incline her slightly towards her back, and let her be held from behind.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until it is full; and let there be a sphere in the vulva from that.
Then let the woman be shaken, and gradually raise her back.  /> For that cotton comes out until there is a sphere before the opening of the vulva, and the broken part returns.
Then place a little down upon its back. When he wants to urinate, let him remove the probe easily, then urinate; and put it back as you first inserted it; then return to his little feather.
Let him do this for seven days, or thereabouts, until the wound heals.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie a straw tube over its opening, then insert the whole bladder into his wound. Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inside the wound swells up, for the bone will return to its place.
Then fill it with a probe and let it remain for several days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when a man's or woman's breastbone is broken, then administer for its restoration and alignment that which we have mentioned concerning the shinbone.
And the rectum should not be hidden in these nearby fractures which hardly touch it, especially for someone who has some learning or experience. â€º
And understand this book of mine well, because the fracture itself indicates to you the right way in many arrangements for its restoration and setting; so understand.
But if a man's penis is broken, take the throat of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place a bandage of cloth over it and let it be wrapped up, and leave it alone for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills it.
Â¶ Concerning the setting of bone fractures when there is also a wound. â€º
It is appropriate for someone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip or thigh bone, to hurry and bleed him immediately: if you find the conditions for bleeding as we have mentioned previously.
If the wound is bleeding, you must hasten to cauterize it, so that you sprinkle ground zegi over it; if nothing else is available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day; and do not delay this, unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to him.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then you must try to reduce it and make it even with your hands, with gentleness and a small amount of stretching.
But if its extension and making it even with your hands are not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight finger-widths; and its width is according to the size of the wound. And because of this it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, according to the quantity needed for that treatment in every type of fracture.
And let it be round; and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not bend during the operation; and let its end be sharp, with an edge at its ends. And let its upper part be thick from half of it to the lower part.
And this is its form.
In this operation, the sharp end should be placed on the protruding bone; and it should be pushed with one thrust until the bone returns and is made even to some extent.
Therefore try to make the ends of the fracture even with each other.
But if the ends of the fracture are fine and the instrument does not take them well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone, until the instrument has power over it.  \\
If you cannot reduce them by the method we have described, then cut him with a suitable instrument from those we mentioned, or saw him with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then scrape off what remains on the bone from roughness and small shells.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced and the patient experiences severe pain after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do this. For you grant great help to the patient when you complete the restoration that you perform on the bone.
Then dip the cloth in black astringent wine. And especially if it is summer. â€º And do not place a fatty substance or anything containing oil on the wound, so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer pills at the hour when you complete the bone's restoration. And leave the wound open: do this by cutting a hole with scissors in the bandages according to the size of the wound. â€º
And be very careful not to bind the wound too tightly over the fracture. Many times indeed have foolish doctors done this and caused either death or gangrene or purulent matter, that is, pus, to occur in their patients. And let your bandage be soft and gentle, different from the binding of other fractures. â€º â€º
If the wound is bad or large and you fear certain evil consequences that we have mentioned, and you find pain in the affected area, then it is not right to place pills on it. And make bandages of hard cloth where the pills would be and bind it with them.
But when it is after one day or two days and you see that the wound has begun to generate pus, then remove from above the cloth which you placed on it with wine. â€º â€º â€º â€º Then administer ointments and unguents with which we are accustomed to treating wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment, and similar ones.
And it is suitable for you to dissolve the bandage. And observe the wound every morning and evening until it heals and consolidates.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that the pus flows down from it easily.
If many days pass and the wound does not heal nor does the pus separate from it, then know that there are small bone fragments there. Therefore, you should examine the wound with a probe. However many fragments are separated out, remove them and extract them.  /// And those that are not separated and cause pain in the limb, making it hurt, strive to cut them out and remove them by whatever means is possible for you.
If the wound becomes inflamed or develops a carbuncle (which is a stye) or another sign of corruption and putrefaction, then you should apply to each of these what is suitable from its treatment, whose description precedes in its chapter.
And concerning what I have said and to which you should pay attention: when a bone is broken and protrudes from the limb, such as a hipbone or thighbone or similar large bones, do not attempt to reduce it, for often death results from this. Instead, leave it alone until it becomes gangrenous.  /// ///
Perhaps indeed it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if it appears to you that you should, and if not, leave it alone.
Â¶ On the treatment of swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling often occurs as a result of treating a fracture. Especially when it is near the joints; and it causes deformity in the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, apply to it remedies that have astringent properties, such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, sarcollege, acacia, and similar substances.
Take some or all of these and prepare them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar; and place them on the swelling over a pad, and bind it firmly with a good bandage.
And leave the bandage in place, without removing it for many days. Then remove it and apply another one, until the swelling is removed. â€»
Or bind over it a sheet of lead neatly. â€» â€» For lead has the property of removing all that is feared in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone and is bound tightly, and there is an urgent need to remove it quickly, then cut a hole in it from above and trim off the prominent excess; or shave it with some razors until it is removed, and care for it until it heals. â€» â€» â€» â€» â€»
Â¶ On the treatment when a limb is restored but remains thin and not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is restored and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this happens only for many reasons. One of these reasons is the frequent loosening and re-tying of the bandage not as required; or because of an excessively tight binding of the ligaments, so that nourishment cannot reach the limb; or because of excessive application of unguents at the wrong time; or because of a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and his weakness. â€»
And the treatment is, to feed the patient and make his body soft, until blood increases in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch on the member, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And frequently apply an embrocation to it with warm water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural shape.
Â¶ On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal crookedly.
When a member already restored after healing becomes twisted or lumpy and the fractured bone has a knot or deformity which disfigures the shape of the member, yet the member does not prevent its natural operation; then it is not necessary to receive the opinion that the member should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish physicians and surgeons who have done this in our land. âˆ™
And indeed this operation is very blameworthy, leading to death or great fear, as if it were deadly. âˆ™
But if the limping and knots are recent, then it is necessary that [the member] be embrocated with warm water in which have been boiled softening herbs, such as althaea leaves, its root, and melilot, and similar ones. âˆ™ ï¸ ï¸ And let there be made for it a softening plaster, such as well-made diachylon.
Or let there be taken the mucilage of the root of althaea and mixed with chicken fat or sesame oil, and let it be plastered with it.
Or let there be taken fat figs and ground with pigeon dung and similar things from the class of drugs which are called diminishing consolidations.
And sometimes the knot is dissolved with frequent, gentle, old friction which is done with wet hands. âˆ™ And movement of the limb should be applied to every part at all hours.
If however the tortuosity has already advanced, and has become strong, and turned into stone, and there is a need to treat it with iron: then it is necessary that its upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be severed, and what is excessive from the knot or the bone be cut away with fine incisions; and ease should be applied to it with diligence and care.
About speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of some joint from its place. Why it is prevented from its movement and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and injuries to happen to the sick person.
Therefore, when someone experiences a dislocation, it is necessary to hasten towards its restoration; and do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps along with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, because of this, it should not be moved or extended during its hour of inflammation.  ?> Because often it causes spasms and painful pains to happen.
But when this happens, then it is necessary that you hasten towards bloodletting of the sick person. Then let it be released until the inflammation quiets down.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil.  ?>  Et tunc oportet, ut sollicite resolvat medicus dislocationem. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And the entire limb should be scraped, along with the part that will come to its place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, following what was mentioned previously about fracture, from the upper part of the body to the lower part of it.
About the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except on rare occasions.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. For either they are removed from their place by a slight displacement and soften slightly. Or they are dislocated completely until they soften to the point of reaching the chest, until the saliva of the patient runs out, and he cannot hold it or close his mouth, and his tongue rolls with speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then it returns to its place by itself in most cases.
But if the dislocation is complete, then it is necessary to administer its reduction quickly and not delay at all: and it is for the assistant to hold the head of the patient: and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between his teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. âž¤âž¤ Or he should insert both thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the other fingers of his hands should be outside, with which he will equalize [the pressure]. âž¤âž¤
And order the patient to clean his jaws and return them to normal chewing in every respect. And the physician should equalize the jaw and push it until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together: then administer steam with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
Therefore, when they return and are equalized, and the patient's mouth is closed and does not soften: then place on them small wads of cloth with a plaster made from wax and rose oil. âž¤âž¤
Then it is bound with ease using soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back; and let his head be supported between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left; nor should he attempt, or be forced, to chew anything; rather, his food should be a gentle drink until the pains are removed, and the jaw is bandaged. Then he may eat what he wishes, and it should be administered with ease.
Let him not strain when eating, drinking, or swallowing, until the jaw is bandaged and healed.
But if the realignment of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their proper places, then frequent fevers and continuous sweating often occur; and perhaps the patient's stomach becomes upset, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this happening, know that it is fatal, and the patient often dies within ten days.
Â¶ On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the end of the humerus.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the inner side because of its connection with the chest. And it is sometimes dislocated to the outside, and this can be perceived by touch.
The restoration of it is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then press upon the place strongly with your palm, for she will return. ã€‹
Then place a poultice and pillows over it, and bind it.
However, the ends of it that follow the elbow and are continuous with it are rarely dislocated. But if it is dislocated on any day, then it should be reduced and set according to what we have said and prepared for you.
Then place a plaster, woolen cloths, and bandages on it. And tell the patient to keep calm and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment the end of the arm can be repositioned when it has also been displaced from its place.
Â¶ On the reduction of shoulder dislocation.
Know that the humerus (upper arm bone) is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is when it dislocates towards the armpit downwards. The second is when it dislocates towards the chest. And perhaps it dislocates towards the upper part of the humerus, and this happens rarely. âŸ©
And it does not dislocate backwards, because of the glenoid cavity. And it does not dislocate forwards, because of the nerves.
Indeed, it most often dislocates and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is scanty. Because it exits quickly in them, and also re-enters quickly. âŸ© âŸ©
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite of this, that is, because it exits with difficulty and also re-enters with difficulty. âŸ© âŸ© âŸ©
And it happens to some people that a blow or fall occurs and the humerus swells with a hot swelling, and it is thought because of this that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you should test this until you are certain about it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And a dislocation is recognized when it is downwards towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. âŸ© âŸ© âŸ© âŸ© For you will find between them a clear difference, and in the head of the humerus there is a concavity, and under the armpit you can feel the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all directions. âŸ© âŸ© âŸ© âŸ© âŸ© âŸ©
And likewise, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then the young person will have a clear sense of touch and it will not be hidden.
And the reduction of this particular dislocation is easy when it is recent or if the child is weak.
The reduction of it is such that the assistant lifts his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under its little finger and raise the joint with strength upwards to its proper place, and the assistant lifts his hand and extends it upwards. Then he places it downward, for it returns quickly by itself.
However, if it does not return through that which we have said, and there are many days since the dislocation first occurred, then it is necessary for the patient to be bathed in warm water and an embrocation should be administered that softens and soothes, such as if althea root, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water and administered.
Then the patient is placed on his back, and a ball and wool are put under his little finger, which should be equal between softness and tightness.  \\
Then the physician places his heel on the ball and lifts the head of the humerus with strength, drawing the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method: and it is as if you make a man taller than the patient stand on his side, and he places his shoulder under the patient's little finger; and he raises the little finger upwards until the patient is suspended in air. And another assistant pulls the patientâ€™s hand down towards his belly. But if the patient is lightweight, then it is necessary to suspend something else with him so that he becomes heavier, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it can also be reduced by another method: and that is, to place a long stick in the ground, whose head is round like the top of a mortar, not too thick, nor too thin. Then let soft cloths be placed under the sick person after he has been laid on top of the head of the stick, and let the sick person stand along the length of the stick.
Then let his hand be extended to the lower part on one side, and let his body be stretched out from the other side with force, for the joint returns quickly to its place.
But if his reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this remedy: and that is, to take a stick whose length is the length of two arms, and its width the width of four fingers, and its thickness the thickness of two fingers; let it have a round head, so that its insertion into the deep part of the armpit can be facilitated according to this shape.
Then let soft cloths be tied over the round head, so that the stick does not hurt the sick person.
Then let it be placed under the sick person's armpit, so that the hand or arm may be extended over the stick down to the lower part; and let the stick be tied over the helper and the arm and the tip of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed across a step of the ladder by its width, and let the hand be extended downward, and let the rest of the body hang suspended on the other side. For the joint returns immediately.
Therefore, when its insertion is completed by any possible method, then it is necessary to place under the armpit a sphere of equal size made from wool. Then apply a plaster prepared from powdered flour of wheat with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is rolled on top.
Then bind a decent bandage under the armpit, and let the bandage be rolled over the plaster on top; and fasten his hand to his own neck, and let it not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the food of the sick person be little, until the part becomes stronger: for it hastens its healing.
Then let there be more [food] after seven or five days, and it should be purged with exercise. If then it becomes firm and not soft, it is already healed.
But if the dislocation is often repeated, either because of moisture that afflicts it or some other cause, then it is necessary to apply a caustic with three holes, as was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
But if all this is done and the ligament is released after seven days and the joint is not strengthened and the plaster and the bandage are repeatedly applied over it and it does not become firm and falls and cannot be lifted upwards, then know that the nerves leading to the head of the shoulder have already been cut or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
However, the dislocation that occurs towards the chest and the breast or backwards is reduced by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns, and then the remaining bandage and care are applied to it until it heals.
But if after healing a heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its motion, then let the sick person use the bath frequently until that heaviness is relieved and returns to its original nature.
Â¶ On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and likewise returns with difficulty. And indeed it can be dislocated in all directions, especially towards the front or the back.
And its dislocation will not be hidden from you, for it is visible and tangible, whatever shape it may take when dislocated. â€º And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is clearly indicated to you. For if the joint were correctly set in place, it would be possible for the arm to bend and to touch its shoulder with it.
And you should hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot inflammation occurs in it.
For if a hot inflammation occurs in it, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocations and causes more intense pain in them; and often death accompanies it.
And its restoration, when it is among those that can be reduced, is as follows: the hand is extended with two hands; and the arm is spread out; and the physician's hands are above the elbow and below it; and he pushes the joint with the thumbs of his hands together or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.  />
Or if the dislocation is towards the front, then it returns in this manner: he folds the hand with his hand until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of his palm.
But if the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a very strong and powerful traction; and it is that two assistants extend the arm; and two assistants also hold him so that he does not move during the traction.
Then the arm is rolled in every direction after a doubled long cloth or wide bandage has been wound around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil, so that this helps in facilitating the slipperiness of the joint. Then he should push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
And after its reduction, an emplaster should be placed on it, in which there is astringency and dryness with the white of an egg; and it should be bound with a moderate bandage. And let the arm be hung from the neck of the sick person: and leave it for some days.
Then release it. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then release the ligature from it and leave it alone.
And if you see that the joint is not well bound, then repeat the bandage and ligature, and leave it alone for some days until it is firmly bound. Then release it.
But if after the reduction of the joint and its setting in motion heaviness occurs, then apply moistening with bathing, subtle friction, and compression until it is straightened, or place over the joint iliati, that is, fatty tallow of a ram. Then let it be bound, and leave it on the joint day and night. Then remove it, and place him in a bath. When he has sweated, then apply moderate friction to the joint. Then repeat on the same place iliati once, and a second time, and a third time upon entering the bath until it is eased.
And if you wish, place on the same cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind it over.
Do this several times. For it is thus eased, and returns to its own state.
Â¶ On the treatment of dislocation according to Al-Zahrawi.
The hand is often dislocated, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
And its reduction is easy. Unlike other joints. ã€‹ Nevertheless, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour when it is dislocated, before the place becomes swollen, or an inflamed swelling occurs there. ã€‹
And the reduction of dislocation is such that you place the hand of the sick person on a board, and let an assistant extend his hand; and the physician places his palm over the prominence of the joint and pushes it until it returns. ã€‹ ã€‹
However, it should be considered whether the palm has already retreated to the abdomen; then the patient should place the back of their hand on a board for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inside on the board, that is, the interior part of his hand should be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls onto that same protuberance of the joint.
If it returns immediately, this is good; and if not, then bind it with a bandage to subdue the swelling, and leave it alone, do not agitate it.
For if he does not tolerate it, and you cannot reduce it, even after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness; and nothing harms the patient except that his hand becomes weakened and cannot grasp anything; then you should know that the nerves are cut or torn.
Therefore, there is no skill required for it, except to bind it with a cautery near the site of the injury. And perhaps this does not help at all.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then place on it the plaster that we have described. Then it should be bound and left for five days; afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
But if its movement is impeded and something heavy happens to it, then soften it with warm water and frequent friction until it becomes softer.
Â¶ On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
When one of the fingers is dislocated in any direction, or if a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm or its abdomen; then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and hang it towards the direction in which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then hang him and extend him until he becomes straight on that day itself.
When it is night, bind him according to the same account: and do not cease to release him during the day; and exercise him with movement; and bind him at night.
Do this for several days until he becomes stiff.
And do the same if his hand is dislocated towards the abdomen; and bind him to that part, and perform your first operation on him until he is healed.
And do the same with him when he is dislocated in other parts.
Â§ On the treatment of spinal dislocation.
When someone experiences a complete dislocation of the spine or neck, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, then there is no cure for it, because death approaches the sick person quickly.
And the sign of this is that the sick person discharges without control and cannot hold it back; and often some of his limbs become flaccid, or both his legs, or both his arms, or one of them; or a single vertebra may be displaced from its position.
For often it is displaced, because often there is a displacement in four parts.
That which is displaced towards the back is called a hump.
But its treatment is that you should consider then, if the hump occurs from childhood; then there is no cure for it, nor any healing at all. />
But in the case of those that occur from an accident, or a blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken about methods of treatment at length. In most cases, it does not return to good health.
But I have already shortened it from him, whose account is excused by my own experience with many of his cases; and in my exposition, which I presented on that topic, and formed an instrument for it in a different way than they demonstrated and explained it.
I say therefore that in a humpback which occurs from the front part, there is no skill nor healing from it.
And similarly in those [humps] that occur on both sides as well.
And it is not cured by these [methods], except for the one that occurs in the back properly, as I shall describe: and it should be done thus â€“ the patient should be stretched out on his face upon a level platform near the wall; and under him should be placed a moist covering, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight piece of wood should be fixed in the ground at the edge of the platform near his head; and another [piece of wood] at the opposite end near his feet. And an assistant should hold the [upper] piece of wood, and it should not be fixed in the hole. And another assistant should hold the other [lower piece of wood].
Then wrap a soft but sturdy bandage around the head of the patient and under his chin; and stretch out the end of the bandage to the piece of wood that is near his head, and tie it securely there.
Then tie another bandage above his ankles and above his knees and at the back of his thighs. Then gather together all the bandages and tie them to the other piece of wood near his feet.
Then let each assistant pull on the bandage toward the piece of wood, and do not remove the two pieces of wood from their places where they are fixed; but they should not be fixed [securely], as we said.
And the doctor should place his hands on the spindle with strength until he returns; or [he may] place a board over it. Then let him attach his feet to the board until he returns.
But if he does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three spans [arms]. And make an opening in the wall near the patient (as I previously said) into which the end of the board can be inserted. /> Then place the middle of the blade onto the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And he should pull firmly until the vertebra is dislocated and then return to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a device that is wound with your hand: and it is as follows: fix two pieces of wood into the ground at the head of the patient, at the far end of the bed, each one arm's length long; and at his head, at the far end of the bed, fix two more pieces of wood.
And let the distance between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place through which the device runs; and let each piece of wood be firmly secured in the ground so that it does not move at all.  />
And insert a round piece of wood; and this is the device on which the band is wound between the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which the wood is secured, whose length is one palm including what is wound; and on the other two pieces of wood it is similar to this.
Then tighten the bands that you have tightened on the patient's chest with the device that is at his head; and the bands that you have tightened on his legs with the device that is at his feet.
Then let there be an assistant standing at each device, who will turn the handle with which the device is wound; and the physician shall straighten the vertebra as we have said.
This is the form of the device that follows.
Then after the vertebra has returned to its place and the area has been straightened, it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then add tow. Then place a splint of wood on top of the plaster, whose width is three fingers or about that. And its length should be such that it can contain the swelling, and extend above a healthy part of the jawbone.
Then let it be tied with the ligature we mentioned. And let the patient use a light diet until healed.
If something of the swelling remains at the end of healing, then it is necessary to administer softening and soothing medicines along with the tablet we mentioned, over a long period of time. And now lead is applied to that plate.
And sometimes a swelling occurs at the end of the spine in the back, and it is thought to be caused by a dislocation, and that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, one should not assume this with that medicine, for perhaps it will cause death.
Â¶ On the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the ankle joint and the shoulder joint do not suffer from slight displacement or indentation like other joints do.
And the ankle joint can be dislocated in four ways. â–¡ This is because it can be dislocated forward and backward.
And indeed it is most commonly dislocated inward, and rarely dislocated forward or backward. â–¡ â–¡ â–¡
And the treatment for its inward dislocation is as follows: when you join the sound leg of the patient to his infirm one, it will be longer; and its knee will protrude more than that of a healthy person, and he cannot bend his foot at the groin; and the place following the groin will be swollen with a hot swelling, because the head of the thigh bone has already reached there.
And the sign of one who has suffered an outward dislocation is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of it [the dislocated bone] is that it extends the healthy leg according to its full length; however, it does not double it without pain in the knee; and if it tries to walk, it cannot step on it with the toes; and urine retention occurs, and the groin swells, and upon walking, there is a stepping on the heel.
And the sign of it [the dislocated bone] occurring to the back is that it does not extend the knee, nor can it double it before doubling the groin; and its leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and there is a prominence at the hip joint.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is, when you consider, if the dislocation is old and has already been present for a long time with the person having it. Then if you perform the reduction on it, and it remains in its position: then there is no treatment for it at all. Therefore, it is not right to make a complete attempt [to reduce it].
But in the case of someone whose dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four types of dislocations: then hasten to do this, so that you roll the joint and extend it towards the inside and outside, and move it from right to left; for perhaps it will return, and will not require any other treatment. ã€‹
But if it does not return this way, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant, and let him extend the leg from below; either with his hands or with a ligature that is tied over the leg above the knee: and another assistant should extend his hand on top, so that he puts his hands under the armpits. ã€‹ Then let him tie it with a gentle ligature around the hip joint, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And let the extension be either from the front part of the groin to the clavicle, or from the back part to the spine.
And let their extension be performed simultaneously, until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is one of the four common kinds of extension.
If then the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every species to do that which I will say about the proper cure.
In the proper reduction of it, when the dislocation is to the interior, it is necessary that the patient be turned over onto his healthy side. Then he should place a bandage over the hip joint, between the head of the femur and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the highest part of the body, to the collarbone area.
Then another strong helper should take hold of his arms and diminish the thick part of the dislocated hip, extending it outward with great force. â€º Now indeed it has returned to its place.
And this kind is the easiest of all the kinds of cure by which this limb is reduced.
But if it resists you and does not obey this cure at all, then it is necessary to bind the two feet of the patient together with a strong, soft bandage over the two heels and over the two knees; and let the length of each be four finger widths from its fellow part: and let the leg of the patient be extended more than one and a half times. â€º
Then let the patient be suspended over the top of a beam that is in the house; and let its length from the ground be two arm lengths.
Then command a strong young man to support the head of the thigh; and let another young man be suspended with the patient; and let another young man push with strength, supporting the thigh, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In its own reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the sick person lies on a bench, as we have narrated concerning those with a hump. And let a ligament be placed above his leg, specifically upon the sick person, and above his chest.
Then two sticks are to be placed: one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a stick be added in the middle of the bench, fixed well; upon which moist cloths are to be wound, so that it does not harm the sick person; and let the stick be between his thighs, so that he is not pulled downward during the stretching.
Then every assistant should stretch to their part, and the physician should balance the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to reduction, all will be well.
And if not, place [something] upon the very table, and press it down, as we have said concerning the hump, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick person lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be quickly stretched; and it is according to this same description upon the bench; and let the physician place his right hand's palm upon the sick person's groin. Then with the other hand he is to squeeze [the leg], and with that squeezing, let him extend it downward to the knee area.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not necessary that the sick person be stretched downward, but rather let him be raised above the ground; indeed, it is necessary that he be placed upon a hard object, just as it is also necessary when his ankle is dislocated outward, according to what we have said concerning his lying upon two ankles: and it is with his face down, and the ligaments stretched, just as we have recently said.
And it is necessary that a bandage be applied with a board also over the place from which the joint exits, when the reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend the two legs of the patient; and when you see them equal, and the patient tightens his leg, and extends it without hesitation, then know that the limb has already returned as it should; then join the two thighs, and apply a plaster, and bind with imemati, that is, a bandage, so that the ankle does not move to any part; and let the patient rest quietly for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in position, then know that the dislocation has been firmly set; therefore release the patient to walk.
And if you see any softness; then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make the patient walk slowly on both [legs] for several days, until it is well strengthened.
Â¶ On the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated to the outside, to the inside, and to the lower part, that is, to the back. ã€‹
And it is not dislocated to the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is, that you tell the patient to apply his leg to his thigh. ã€‹ ã€‹ ã€‹ If it does not adhere with the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the reduction of all kinds of dislocation is, that you make the patient sit upright, so that he can already extend his leg over it, if he is able, and let your back be against his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make the knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers on his knee. Then apply both sides of the knee with your palm firmly. And another assistant should extend the foot until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of a successful reduction is that the leg adheres gently with the hip, not forcefully.
Then apply a plaster to it, and make the leg adhere with the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then unbind them.
And the patient should not walk for some days until he is healed.
But if the reduction of it is resisted, then apply strong traction with bands, as was previously described in the treatment of the ankle, until it returns to its place.
Â¶ On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Heel.
The heel is sometimes displaced by a slight movement, and sometimes it is completely dislocated. And its dislocation occurs either to the inside or to the outside. â€º
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has been dislocated.
The treatment of its slight displacement is easy reduction; and that is to extend it gently with the hands and equalize it until it returns.
But the treatment of its complete dislocation requires that you make the patient sit upright, and have a strong assistant hold him firmly from behind his back in the middle of it.
Then take hold of his foot with your right hand from above it, and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the heel. â€º
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with your left hand, two times.
Then extend with the left hand, and push the foot's chest with the right hand towards the leg without effort.
Do this twice, as we have told you.
Then push the foot's chest towards the leg a third time, and you will draw out the vein.
If therefore it returns once or twice according to this method, and you see the foot is even, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if its reduction is prevented from you according to what we have told you, then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a nail in the ground very firmly, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have already wrapped a cloth around him, so that it does not hurt the sick person.
Then let the assistant take hold of his thigh; afterwards let the other assistant stretch the foot, either with his hands or with a band that binds around the neck of the foot. Then let every assistant stretch against the extension of his fellow; and let the nail stand between the two thighs of the sick person, holding him so that his body is not drawn downwards when stretched.
Then let the physician equalize the dislocation with his hands, and let the other assistant hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns swiftly.
When therefore the dislocation returns and its healthful reduction is made clear to you, then place a plaster and wool, and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be careful or fearful of the nerves that are above the heel in the back, so that the bandage is not too tight on them and hurts them.
Then leave him for two days, or three. But if the bandage becomes loose, bind it; then unbind it on the third day, or the fourth; and forbid the sick person from walking for forty days. If indeed he attempts to walk before that time, security is not achieved without the dislocation breaking up and becoming corrupt, and it will not receive cure afterwards.
If a hot abscess occurs: then it is necessary that you administer to him the treatment mentioned earlier in another section on healing, and a poultice, until it is removed.
About the dislocation of the toes and their cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them be put back into place with a small extension without difficulty; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in certain nodes of the back of the foot, then it is necessary that the patient sit with his foot on an even place on the ground or on a table, and stand as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from those joints. ã€‹ Then press it with your foot firmly until it returns and you see it is already in place, and no prominence appears in that place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that fits the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then unbind him, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is bound and secured in its place. ã€‹
About the types of dislocations with a wound or a fracture.
When something like this happens and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows; therefore one should not presume to undertake such a cure unless he is skilled in the art after long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not reckless or presumptuous; and that he use at the beginning of the treatment only those medicines which suppress hot abscesses: and leave the patient to divine judgment. ã€‹ ã€‹ Unless you see or hope for safety from destruction with the mildness of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, try to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess forms.
If the member returns as we wish, then administer a treatment that calms the abscess; and care for the wound with one of the drying ointments suitable for it.
If the dislocation is with a fracture and separate pieces occur in the bone, then try to remove them; and do as we said in simple diseases from what precedes in their places.
And be careful according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear; as my prediction precedes for you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more enduring to your glory, and safer for your body among them at your time.
